</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="Abs1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="Par1">With the construction of the International Space Station, humans have been continuously living and working in space for 22 years. Microbial studies in space and other extreme environments on Earth have shown the ability for bacteria and fungi to adapt and change compared to “normal” conditions. Some of these changes, like biofilm formation, can impact astronaut health and spacecraft integrity in a negative way, while others, such as a propensity for plastic degradation, can promote self-sufficiency and sustainability in space. With the next era of space exploration upon us, which will see crewed missions to the Moon and Mars in the next 10 years, incorporating microbiology research into planning, decision-making, and mission design will be paramount to ensuring success of these long-duration missions. These can include astronaut microbiome studies to protect against infections, immune system dysfunction and bone deterioration, or biological in situ resource utilization (bISRU) studies that incorporate microbes to act as radiation shields, create electricity and establish robust plant habitats for fresh food and recycling of waste. In this review, information will be presented on the beneficial use of microbes in bioregenerative life support systems, their applicability to bISRU, and their capability to be genetically engineered for biotechnological space applications. In addition, we discuss the negative effect microbes and microbial communities may have on long-duration space travel and provide mitigation strategies to reduce their impact. Utilizing the benefits of microbes, while understanding their limitations, will help us explore deeper into space and develop sustainable human habitats on the Moon, Mars and beyond.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Subject terms:</strong> Microbiology, Biotechnology, Biological techniques</p></section></section><section id="Sec1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p id="Par2">The National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA) has pledged to return humans to the Moon in the next two years and land the first humans on Mars by 2033. The journey beyond low Earth orbit (LEO) will expand human civilization, enable future space settlements, provide scientific knowledge of the evolution of our planet and the solar system, and create global partnerships in the quest for further space exploration<sup><a href="#CR1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR1">1</a>,<a href="#CR2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR2">2</a></sup>. Under the Artemis plan, a crewed lunar flyby is scheduled for 2024 (Artemis II), followed by a lunar landing in 2025 (Artemis III)—the first since the end of the Apollo era in 1972, and eventually a sustainable lunar presence by the end of this decade<sup><a href="#CR3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR3">3</a></sup>. Critical to the success of the Artemis program will be Gateway, an orbiting platform where astronauts will live and conduct research, while providing support for lengthy expeditions on the lunar surface. The Artemis program will establish a base camp at the lunar south pole that will serve as a steppingstone for human missions to Mars. Research and development at the lunar base will act as prototypes for these future Mars missions, where NASA can establish best practices for long-term human exploration in these adverse extraterrestrial environments<sup><a href="#CR4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR4">4</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par3">Unlike the operation of the International Space Station (ISS), which is regularly resupplied from Earth within hours after launch, deep space missions will require self-sufficiency and sustainability independent of Earth. This will involve utilization of renewable resources, recycling of waste, power generation, and a continuous supply of food, water, and oxygen over a prolonged/indefinite period. The moon is the shortest distance beyond LEO with a deep space environment offering unique research opportunities to be conducted under the Artemis program. The lunar orbiter Gateway will function similarly to the ISS utilizing a Power and Propulsion Element that will use solar energy to propel and power the spacecraft, a Habitation and Logistics Outpost that will serve as the living quarters and research workspace, and docking ports for spacecraft such as Orion, that will be the first of its kind to transport astronauts to and from deep space<sup><a href="#CR5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR5">5</a>,<a href="#CR6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR6">6</a></sup>. The ISS and Earth-orbiting satellites capitalize on solar energy as a renewable resource for power, however in more distant outposts such as Mars, other factors like distance from the sun, angle, and weather (i.e., dust storms) affect the efficiency of energy provided by the solar arrays<sup><a href="#CR7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR7">7</a></sup>. Such was the case with NASA’s Insight mission, where a recent Martian dust storm led to accumulated dust on the solar panels preventing adequate sunlight from reaching them, forcing the lander into battery-conserving “safe mode”<sup><a href="#CR8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR8">8</a></sup>. Similar dust coverage issues were experienced during Apollo missions due to electrically charged lunar dust adhering to solar panels on the lunar lander<sup><a href="#CR9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR9">9</a>,<a href="#CR10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR10">10</a></sup>. Resupply cargo, like those that are frequently sent to the ISS, is costly, and may not be feasible for long-duration space missions (it takes ~7 months to get to Mars). Thus, self-sustainability in food and oxygen production on extraterrestrial outposts, such as on the Moon and Mars, is crucial<sup><a href="#CR11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR11">11</a></sup>. In addition, communication delays between Earth and Mars can range from 5 to 20 min depending on the position of the planets<sup><a href="#CR12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR12">12</a></sup>. Lack of cargo resupply missions and communication delays can be detrimental to human health-related emergencies making it imperative for crew members to be self-sufficient in health risk prevention and treatment. Therefore, solutions to address limited resources and human health risks that can be feasibly implemented in deep space must be established prior to the Artemis and Mars exploration missions. This could be achieved through the exploitation and engineering of microbes important to human health<sup><a href="#CR13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR13">13</a>–<a href="#CR16" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR16">16</a></sup>, agriculture<sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup>, food production<sup><a href="#CR18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR18">18</a>–<a href="#CR20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR20">20</a></sup>, the ecosystem<sup><a href="#CR21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR21">21</a>–<a href="#CR25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR25">25</a></sup>, and the built environment<sup><a href="#CR26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR26">26</a>,<a href="#CR27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR27">27</a></sup>. Figure <a href="#Fig1" class="usa-link">1</a> provides an overview of the various roles microbes could play in deep space exploration.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig1"><h3 class="obj_head">Fig. 1. Summary of microbial impact during long-duration space missions.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10284894_41526_2023_285_Fig1_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/ad26/10284894/9f00e0a4865f/41526_2023_285_Fig1_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d32e436" height="716" width="748" alt="Fig. 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Space exploration can benefit from the use of microbes in a variety of applications including incorporation into biological life support systems (BLSS), in situ resource utilization beyond LEO, and astronaut therapeutics. However, increased pathogenicity and biofilm formation during spaceflight could threaten astronaut health and spacecraft integrity so mitigation strategies will be needed to prevent such hazards. Microbial applications related to health (purple), renewable resources (green) or both (purple and green) are highlighted. Figure created with BioRender.com.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par4">In this review, we will examine some key considerations for planning crewed space missions that allow for self-sufficiency and sustainability and specifically the role that microbes can play in achieving these goals. We will also discuss the possible detrimental effects of microbes that could derail a mission, such as biofouling and increased pathogenicity, and suggest mitigation strategies to help alleviate some of these concerns.</p></section><section id="Sec2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Bioregenerative life support systems and the value of microbial inclusion</h2>
<p id="Par5">NASA has been sending astronauts to space for the last 60 years, and with the advent of deep space missions to the Moon and Mars, astronauts must be self-sufficient to provide atmospheric conditions and necessities for human life (i.e., purified water and nutrient-rich food)<sup><a href="#CR28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR28">28</a>,<a href="#CR29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR29">29</a></sup>. This self-sufficiency can be achieved by implementing bioregenerative life support systems (BLSS). BLSS generate essential resources for human survival through biological processes, with four main purposes: higher plant cultivation, water treatment, solid waste bioconversion, and atmosphere revitalization. Microbes play a vital role in these biological processes by reducing the storage and resupply of materials necessary for a life-sustaining, regenerative environment.</p>
<p id="Par6">Research on BLSS dates back to as early as the 1960s, by researchers worldwide. Examples include NASA’s “BioHome,” a 650 sq ft closed system that utilized a wetland system for wastewater recycling, and a biological system including plants and microorganisms for reducing organic contamination from wastewater<sup><a href="#CR29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR29">29</a></sup>; the Soviet space program’s Biosphere 3 (BIOS-3), an underground closed system of phytotrons, that consists of a crew area and an entirely enclosed greenhouse, growing wheat and vegetables as well as algae cultivators for air revitalization<sup><a href="#CR30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR30">30</a></sup>; the European Space Agency’s (ESA) Micro-Ecological Life Support System Alternative (MELiSSA), which includes five compartments where plants and microorganisms purify the air, produce food, and recycle waste<sup><a href="#CR31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR31">31</a></sup>; and Beighan University’s Lunar Permanent Astrobase Life-support Artificial Closed Ecosystem (Palace) 1, comprising three cabins that work simultaneously to manage atmospheric conditions, produce crops, breed insects, and recover solid and liquid waste<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a></sup>. In all these support systems, microbes are an essential component in the regulation, degradation and circulation of materials and energy, ultimately enhancing the effectiveness of these life support systems.</p>
<section id="Sec3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Plant cultivation</h3>
<p id="Par7">Research and development of higher plant cultivation, a method of growing crops with increased productivity, enhanced nutritional value, higher volume utilization, and shorter production cycle, are necessary for the development of sustainable ecosystems in space. Higher plant cultivation modules within BLSS not only provide a means for food production but also the recycling and revitalization of air through photosynthesis, and water recycling through transpiration and waste management<sup><a href="#CR33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR33">33</a></sup>. Because of their importance, plant modules, and the effects of extraterrestrial conditions on plant growth have been extensively studied<sup><a href="#CR34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR34">34</a>–<a href="#CR36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR36">36</a></sup>. For example, NASA is heavily involved in this area of research with its Advanced Plant Habitat (APH) and Vegetable Production System (Veggie) experiments on the ISS. Both APH and Veggie are automated growth chambers used by researchers to determine the effects of microgravity on plant gene expression, protein, and metabolite levels, as well as their nutritional value<sup><a href="#CR37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR37">37</a></sup>. The Veggie system has successfully grown lettuce, Chinese cabbage, mizuna mustard, red Russian kale, and zinnia flowers on the ISS<sup><a href="#CR37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR37">37</a></sup> and has enhanced our understanding of plant-microbe-environment interactions in microgravity<sup><a href="#CR38" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR38">38</a></sup>. Using the Veggie system, Hummerick et al. characterized microorganisms found on the leaves of three leafy greens: red romaine lettuce, mizuna mustard, and green leaf lettuce, as well as the microbial communities associated with the rhizosphere in the pillow component<sup><a href="#CR39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR39">39</a></sup>. Characterization revealed higher microbial diversity near roots and within root substrate compared to leaves, consistent with plants grown in terrestrial soils. Molecular and culture-based methods revealed that the crops were pathogen-free and safe for human consumption. The information gained from the APH and Veggie experiments, especially as it pertains to plant-microbe interactions, provide a foundation for future research on higher plant cultivation in microgravity and the expansion of these ideas for plant production on extraterrestrial outposts.</p>
<p id="Par8">One issue with hydroponic growth systems on Earth is microbial contamination, most often by <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em><sup><a href="#CR40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR40">40</a></sup>. Certain abiotic conditions such as high humidity, high temperature, and reduced airflow can cause undesirably high levels of microbial growth<sup><a href="#CR41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR41">41</a>,<a href="#CR42" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR42">42</a></sup>. Veggie is a hydroponic system that has also succumbed to these limitations. <em>Zinnia hybrida</em>, an annual flowering plant, grown in the Veggie system on the ISS, developed foliar, stem, and root rot disease, due to high water stress and low airflow<sup><a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">43</a></sup>. Whole genome sequencing analysis of the diseased tissue<sup><a href="#CR44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR44">44</a></sup> and subsequent virulence assays<sup><a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">43</a></sup>, identified the culprit as <em>F. oxysporum</em>. This illustrates the potential difficulties of growing crops in hydroponic systems, on Earth or in space.</p>
<p id="Par9">An alternative to a hydroponic system is a soil-based one where Martian and lunar regolith can be used as an alternative to terrestrial soil. This in situ resource utilization (ISRU) of regolith would reduce the need for costly resupply missions of terrestrial soil. While many plants and crops have been successfully grown in simulated Martian and lunar regolith their characteristics do differ from what would be expected with terrestrial soil<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a>–<a href="#CR49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR49">49</a></sup>. One limiting factor of regolith is the absence of reactive nitrogen, an essential nutrient for optimal plant growth and function<sup><a href="#CR50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR50">50</a>–<a href="#CR54" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR54">54</a></sup>. However, the introduction of nitrogen-fixing and nitrogen-cycling bacterial species into regolith to bind nitrogen from the atmosphere and transform it into reactive nitrogen (in the form of NO3− and NH4+) could be used as a method to improve regolith soil fertility<sup><a href="#CR55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR55">55</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par10">Increased Martian soil fertility through symbiotic relationships has been examined with clover (<em>Melilotus officinalis</em>), grown in simulated regolith that had been inoculated with the nitrogen-fixing bacterium, <em>Sinorhizobium meliloti</em><sup><a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">56</a></sup>. This study found that after three months, inoculated treatments produced greater clover biomass compared to uninoculated treatments, 0.29 g and 0.01 g, respectively. However, when <em>S. meliloti</em> inoculated clover was grown in common terrestrial potting mix the total clover biomass was seven-fold greater than when grown in simulated regolith<sup><a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">56</a></sup>. While plant-bacterial symbiosis could improve soil fertility and plant growth in Martian regolith, additional experiments are required to achieve terrestrial levels of plant biomass.</p>
<p id="Par11">Other plant stressors, such as limited nutrients, may prevent plants from reaching optimal biomass<sup><a href="#CR56" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR56">56</a></sup>. Essential nutrients, such as potassium, calcium, magnesium, iron, manganese, nickel, and zinc, are present in extraterrestrial soils but not at sufficient levels for plant uptake. Zaets et al. showed that bacteria can increase the bioavailability of these minerals in simulated regolith using inoculants of <em>Pseudomonas sp</em>. IMBG163, <em>Pseudomonas aureofaciens</em> IMBG164, <em>Stenotrophomonas maltophilia</em> IMBG147, <em>Paenibacillus sp</em>. IMBG156, <em>Klebsiella oxytoca</em> IMBG26, and <em>Pantoea agglomerans</em> IMV<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a></sup>. When inoculated with these bacteria, increased bioavailability of essential nutrients in the soil and plant tissue of <em>Tagetes patula</em> (i.e., French marigold) led to increased plant growth, seed germination and survival<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a></sup>. Conversely, only 20–30% of plants grown in non-inoculated soils achieved seed germination<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a></sup>. In addition to increasing nutrient bioavailability, these bacteria were also able to reduce toxic levels of zinc, chromium, nickel, iron, calcium, and sodium, by up to 50%, within plant tissue<sup><a href="#CR45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR45">45</a></sup>. By increasing nutrient availability and reducing toxic accumulation of ions within the soil, microorganisms can be used as a tool for conditioning Martian and lunar basalt for effective plant growth and plant nutrient uptake.</p>
<p id="Par12">Water is another crucial plant resource limited on both the Moon and Mars. Previous discoveries have found evidence of liquid water flows on Mars, coming from giant ice slabs beneath the surface<sup><a href="#CR57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR57">57</a></sup>, though extracting and recycling water from these ice slabs is energetically impractical. In addition, Martian soil has limited water-holding capacity due to low organic carbon content, however, this can be improved by using bacteria that produce polysaccharides or adhesive proteins that bind soil particles, thereby increasing the moisture content of soil<sup><a href="#CR58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR58">58</a></sup>. This microbe-soil interaction can be exploited on Martian outposts to reduce the need for copious amounts of water, increase soil stability, and prevent soil desiccation. Several studies on agricultural soils show that the application of microalgae and cyanobacteria to the soil can improve soil fertility and health<sup><a href="#CR59" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR59">59</a>–<a href="#CR61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR61">61</a></sup>. Nascimento et al. assessed the ability of the N-fixing cyanobacteria <em>Nostoc sp</em>. to act as an organic fertilizer and soil conditioner under normal and drought conditions<sup><a href="#CR60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR60">60</a></sup>. Researchers applied <em>Nostoc sp</em>. and urea as liquid fertilizers to soil growing wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum</em>), corn (<em>Zea mays</em>), and common bean (<em>Phaseolus vulgaris</em>). Drought conditions were simulated by watering the plant to water holding capacity and drying the soil for 14–16 days. Under drought conditions, plants fertilized with <em>Nostoc sp</em>. reached a biomass 150% greater than plants continuously watered to water holding capacity; while plants fertilized only with urea attained only 70% of the biomass compared to those continuously held at water holding capacity. Researchers also found that untreated soils exhibited more leaf wilting from water stress compared to those plants grown in soil treated with <em>Nostoc sp</em><sup><a href="#CR60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR60">60</a></sup>. This research shows the promise that cyanobacteria can have for improving soil quality for plant growth beyond LEO.</p>
<p id="Par13">While Martian and lunar regolith are promising soil sources, they contain heavy metals, such as lead, cadmium, chromium, and arsenic, that can negatively impact plant growth and soil microbial fitness<sup><a href="#CR62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR62">62</a>,<a href="#CR63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR63">63</a></sup>. Microbes can be used for bioremediation to convert Martian and lunar regolith into soil capable of plant growth<sup><a href="#CR64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR64">64</a>–<a href="#CR66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR66">66</a></sup>. Huang et al. tested the ability of <em>E. coli</em> and <em>B. subtilis</em> to remove lead, cadmium, and chromium by cultivating samples in solutions containing varying heavy metal concentrations and environmental conditions, including pH, temperature, and equilibration time. Researchers found that both microbes successfully removed heavy metals under all conditions, though under optimal conditions, <em>E. coli</em> removed 60–69% of cadmium, lead, and chromium while <em>B. subtilis</em> removed 54–70% of cadmium, lead, and chromium<sup><a href="#CR67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR67">67</a></sup>. Plant-microorganism interactions can also be a source of bioremediation by using plant growth-promoting rhizobacteria that can simultaneously remove toxic heavy metals and improve crop growth and yield<sup><a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">68</a></sup>. Henao and Ghneim-Herrera investigated this bioremediation method by summarizing results from over 85 research articles and found that <em>Acinetobacter</em>, <em>Agrobacterium</em>, <em>Arthrobacter, Bacillus</em>, <em>Enterobacter</em>, <em>Klebsiella</em>, <em>Mesorhizobium</em>, <em>Microbacterium</em>, <em>Pseudomonas</em>, <em>Rhizobium</em>, <em>Rhodococcus</em>, and <em>Variovorax</em> all exhibited resistance to heavy metals and a high potential for bioremediation. Specifically, <em>Klebsiella</em> and <em>Enterobacter</em> exhibited the highest tolerance to heavy metals in soil and the greatest potential to mitigate plant growth inhibition under high arsenic, cadmium, and lead concentrations<sup><a href="#CR68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR68">68</a></sup>. These results are mirrored by Yetunde Mutiat et al. <sup><a href="#CR69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR69">69</a></sup>, who assessed the removal efficiency of heavy metals under varying pH levels by wild-type and mutant strains of <em>Klebisella varicola</em>. Isolated <em>Klebisella</em> strains were exposed to various concentrations of lead, cadmium, arsenic, and nickel, resulting in removal of cadmium under all conditions with a maximum removal efficiency of 97.9 and 99.4% at optimal conditions of pH 7 for both wild-type and mutant strains.</p>
<p id="Par14">Microbes can also be used to remove toxins from Martian soil such as perchlorates, which are found in high levels in Martian soil and cause a significant reduction in plant survival and productivity<sup><a href="#CR70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR70">70</a>,<a href="#CR71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR71">71</a></sup>. Engineered CO<sub>2</sub>-utilizing bacteria expressing perchlorate reduction enzymes have been shown to remove harmful perchlorates from the soil while also adding essential nutrients into the soil, such as chloride ions, oxygen, and water for better plant growth<sup><a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">72</a>–<a href="#CR74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR74">74</a></sup>. Sunikumar et al. tested the ability of two perchlorate-reducing soil bacteria, <em>Pseudomonas stutzeri</em> and <em>Azospirillum brasilense</em>, to reduce perchlorates from simulated regolith and found that they removed up to 5 mM and 10 mM of perchlorates, respectively, which corresponded to a removal efficiency of 100%<sup><a href="#CR75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR75">75</a></sup>. These results suggest that naturally occurring or genetically engineered microbes with high perchlorate and/or toxin-reducing efficiency should be further studied for bioremediation of perchlorate and other harmful toxins from Martian and lunar soils.</p>
<p id="Par15">Just as microorganisms are a vital part of terrestrial plant production systems, microorganisms will play an important role in higher plant production and soil systems on future deep space missions and extraterrestrial outposts. Previous research indicates that plant production using hydroponic systems is a promising method for plant production in microgravity<sup><a href="#CR34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR34">34</a>–<a href="#CR36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR36">36</a></sup>, but further optimization will be required to prevent fungal contamination in these systems<sup><a href="#CR43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR43">43</a></sup>. Using soil-based plant growth systems is a promising alternative to circumvent the limitations of hydroponics, but research is limited in this area within BLSS. Therefore, further research using soil-based plant growth systems, supplemented with microorganisms, may improve the effectiveness of BLSS and self-sufficiency of astronauts on deep space missions.</p></section><section id="Sec4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Wastewater treatment</h3>
<p id="Par16">Water is the largest product consumed in bioregenerative systems, expending nearly 20 L per person per day<sup><a href="#CR76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR76">76</a></sup>. Extensive water consumption results in large wastewater production, including urine and flush water, atmospheric condensate, sink, shower, laundry, and dish water. Microbes play a vital role in the recycling of wastewater and nutrients through recycling systems containing combinations of anaerobic digestion, distillation, and disinfectant units.</p>
<p id="Par17">Microbes also play a crucial role in solid waste processing (including bodily waste), inedible plant material, and other solid decomposable substances within bioregenerative systems. Drying is the first step to recycling solid waste<sup><a href="#CR30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR30">30</a>,<a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a>,<a href="#CR77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR77">77</a></sup>. This step allows the extraction of water from solid waste, the retention of organic matter, and the removal of inorganic material<sup><a href="#CR78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR78">78</a></sup>. Dried, solid waste is then fermented in a solid waste bioreactor containing microbes that degrade plant waste<sup><a href="#CR32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR32">32</a>,<a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a>,<a href="#CR80" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR80">80</a></sup>. This method has shown solid waste degradation rates between 41% and 87.7%<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a></sup>. The degraded solid waste can either be taken out of the system or applied to a plant system, providing a carbon and nitrogen-rich source of residue fertilizer or soil-like substance that increases soil fertility and overall plant health and productivity<sup><a href="#CR81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR81">81</a>–<a href="#CR83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR83">83</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par18">There are many proposed systems for microbe-assisted waste purification and recycling on spacecraft. The MELiSSA initiative proposed a loop of compartments that thoroughly recycle gas, liquid, and solid waste using microorganisms, where each output of the preceding compartment provides the input for the following compartment<sup><a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">84</a></sup>. Compartment I is an anaerobic digester that utilizes thermophilic bacteria to break down inedible plant parts and solid and liquid waste. <em>Clostridium thermocellum</em> ferments cellulosic substrate, while <em>Clostridium thermosaccharolyticum</em> degrades starches and pectins, leaving volatile fatty acids, minerals and NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> as an output. In compartment II, photoheterotrophic bacteria, such as <em>Rhodospirillum rubrum</em>, metabolize volatile fatty acids. The remaining minerals and NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> enter compartment III where nitrifying bacteria, such as those in the species <em>Nitrobacter</em> or <em>Nitrosomonas</em>, nitrify NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> to NO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, which can be utilized in the plant compartment as a fertilizer<sup><a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">84</a></sup>. Overall, this system results in a nitrogen-rich output that can be utilized as fertilizer in the plant compartment for improved production.</p>
<p id="Par19">Another system proposed by Tang et al. utilizes a two-system recycling unit for either domestic water or wastewater<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a></sup>. Domestic water is purified by first running it through a two-stage membrane bioreactor and then passing it through a nanofiltration system, to produce hygiene water. The second system utilizes anaerobic, mostly Bacteroidetes, and aerobic, mostly Proteobacteria, microbial bioreactors to recover organic matter and N from wastewater<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a></sup>. Within this system, microorganisms are also utilized to degrade solid waste as part of the microbial fermentation facility or Bio-toilet. The facility includes a source separation module that separates urine from feces, a primary bioreactor where feces are combined with other inedible plant material to be degraded by microorganisms, and a secondary bioreactor for further degradation by microbes. This system was tested during 108-day experiment housing four crew members at the China Astronaut Research and Training Center. Researchers achieved 100% water regeneration with 87.7% recycled solid waste<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a>,<a href="#CR80" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR80">80</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par20">Although BLSS can obtain 100% water recovery, nitrogen recovery efficiency is still lacking. One option to improve nitrogen recovery is to utilize urease-producing microorganisms to hydrolyze urea, a compound found in human urine at high levels (&gt;13 g/L)<sup><a href="#CR85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR85">85</a>,<a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">86</a></sup>. Urease-producing microorganisms, such as <em>Bacillus, Sporosarcina, Pseudomonas</em>, and <em>Paracoccus</em>, used in conjunction with membrane-biological activated carbon reactor systems by Xie et al. showed that BLSS can obtain water recovery of 100% with N recovery of up to 79.33%, which are comparable to efficiencies obtained by Tang et al.<sup><a href="#CR79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR79">79</a></sup>. Another urine-fueled system for waste recycling, proposed by Maggi et al., includes a soil-based BLSS aimed at recycling liquid wastes using a plant-microbe system<sup><a href="#CR87" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR87">87</a></sup>. The growth chambers for dwarf wheat and soybean contain three systems for water and urine injection, atmospheric circulation, and ventilation. Once injected into the soil, a number of bacteria can release nitrogen-based intermediates, such as NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> and NO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> from organic nitrogen compounds for plants to uptake. Results indicated that urine decomposition met the nutrient demands of the plants as evidenced by successful growth of the dwarf wheat and soybean plants with comparable biomass generation to those grown on Earth.</p>
<p id="Par21">Plant-microbe systems can provide other methods of wastewater recycling. Plants are excellent water purifiers and can release 2–10 L of water vapor from their leaves through the process of transpiration<sup><a href="#CR88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR88">88</a></sup>. Plants uptake water through their roots, absorb nutrients into plant tissue, and transpire water through their stomata. Applying wastewater as a means of watering plants would effectively turn wastewater into clean water through this natural process. However, before plants can be exposed to wastewater, it would need to be pre-treated to reduce organic loading in soil and remove phytotoxic or other detrimental compounds that would affect plant growth and metabolism<sup><a href="#CR89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR89">89</a>,<a href="#CR90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR90">90</a></sup>. This can be achieved with microbial bioreactors through the mechanisms described above, allowing for eco-friendly water reclamation.</p></section><section id="Sec5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Atmosphere revitalization</h3>
<p id="Par22">It is projected that crew members on a lunar mission will inhale about 1 kg of O<sub>2</sub> per day and exhale approximately 1.3 kg of CO<sub>2</sub><sup><a href="#CR91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR91">91</a></sup>. Production of O<sub>2</sub> and removal of CO<sub>2</sub> during space missions could be achieved through photosynthesis, the process by which plants, algae and cyanobacteria convert CO<sub>2</sub>, sunlight, and water, into O<sub>2</sub> and energy<sup><a href="#CR92" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR92">92</a></sup>. Cyanobacteria are the earliest oxygenic photosynthetic organisms on Earth and have been contributing to Earth’s atmospheric oxygen for the last 2.5 billion years<sup><a href="#CR93" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR93">93</a>,<a href="#CR94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR94">94</a></sup>. One advantage of using cyanobacteria over plants for air revitalization is their ability to perform photosynthesis with far less sunlight than is required for plant growth. Under normal conditions, plants and cyanobacteria use chlorophyll-<em>a</em> to convert visible (i.e. “white”) light into energy, but some cyanobacteria can perform far-red photosynthesis, using chlorophyll-<em>f</em>, a spectrally red-shifted variant of chlorophyll-<em>a</em> which absorbs longer wavelengths of light<sup><a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a>–<a href="#CR97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR97">97</a></sup>. This allows those cyanobacteria to also perform photosynthesis and harvest energy when grown in low- or filtered- light environments<sup><a href="#CR95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR95">95</a>–<a href="#CR97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR97">97</a></sup>. This photosynthetic efficiency, coupled with the ability to survive the harsh conditions of space<sup><a href="#CR98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR98">98</a>–<a href="#CR101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR101">101</a></sup> make cyanobacteria ideal components in BLSS destined for the Moon and Mars.</p>
<p id="Par23">Photobioreactors can be incorporated into BLSS to increase the production of oxygen by cyanobacteria or algae for enhanced air revitalization. ESA’s MELiSSA project is a BLSS concept focused on the regeneration of atmospheric gases and water, waste treatment, and food production for crewed space missions<sup><a href="#CR102" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR102">102</a>,<a href="#CR103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR103">103</a></sup>. The system comprises the listed compartments, each with a specific organism contributing to the recycling pathway<sup><a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">104</a></sup>. One of the five compartments includes a gas-lift photobioreactor containing photosynthetic cyanobacteria, specifically <em>Spirulina platensis</em>, that uses the CO<sub>2</sub> produced by its predecessor compartment to produce oxygen<sup><a href="#CR84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR84">84</a></sup>. <em>S. platensis</em> was chosen for its light energy conversion efficiency, its ability to tolerate fluctuations in pH, and its high nutritional value (containing 55–70% protein, 15–25% carbohydrates, 18% essential fatty acids in addition to vitamins, minerals, and pigments<sup><a href="#CR105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR105">105</a></sup>). Another species of cyanobacteria that is being considered for air revitalization, nitrate removal and edible biomass production in MELiSSA is <em>Limnospira indica</em>. In a recent 35-day ground study, <em>L. indica</em> was grown in a simplified closed-loop version of MELiSSA and the effect of urea, ammonium (the prominent nitrogen forms present in non-nitrified urine) and nitrate, on the oxygen production capacity of <em>L. indica</em>, was measured<sup><a href="#CR106" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR106">106</a></sup>. It was observed that cyanobacteria fed nitrate or urea could effectively reach the desired (set point) O<sub>2</sub> level of 20.3% and maintain ambient O<sub>2</sub> levels, while those fed ammonium could only reach a maximum O<sub>2</sub> level of 19.5%<sup><a href="#CR106" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR106">106</a></sup>. This study provided preliminary evidence for the use of ammonium-rich and urea-rich media (such as urine), for <em>L. indica</em> cultivation and air revitalization. <em>L. indica</em> has also been grown in photobioreactors on the ISS, as part of the Arthrospira-B spaceflight experiment, and no inhibitory effect on oxygen production and growth was observed, as compared to ground controls<sup><a href="#CR107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR107">107</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par24">These studies show the promise of cyanobacteria-based BLSS and/or photobioreactors destined for the Moon and Mars to provide clean air for crew in spacecraft or in lunar/Mars habitats. Additional research is needed for optimization such as identifying additional candidate species, growing combinations of different cyanobacteria for synergistic effects, and testing more growth conditions to achieve enhanced biomass and increased efficiency.</p></section></section><section id="Sec6"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Biological in situ resource utilization for sustainability</h2>
<p id="Par25">In addition to BLSS which can increase self-sufficiency and sustainability beyond LEO, the ability to utilize in situ resources, will also play a role in long-term human habitats on the Moon and Mars. For instance, electricity and power can be generated with microbial fuel cells (MFC) coupled with in situ organic material, and biomining can be used to extract resources for construction, repair, and maintenance of structural components and equipment.</p>
<section id="Sec7"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Microbial fuel cells</h3>
<p id="Par26">Microbial production of energy has gained much interest in the last decade. To keep pace with human energy consumption, many scientists have turned towards the use of microbial fuel cells as a sustainable method of energy production on Earth<sup><a href="#CR108" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR108">108</a></sup>. These alternative methods of energy production could also be applied for space exploration as a sustainable method to power the spacecraft, mission controls, and various life support systems.</p>
<p id="Par27">MFC are small, lightweight devices that convert organic matter from renewable sources into electricity using microorganisms as catalysts<sup><a href="#CR109" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR109">109</a></sup> (Fig. <a href="#Fig2" class="usa-link">2</a>). Microorganisms involved in this electrochemical activity are called exoelectrogens because of their ability to transfer electrons exogenously to electron acceptors<sup><a href="#CR109" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR109">109</a></sup>. Some examples of exoelectrogens include <em>Pseudomonas</em><sup><a href="#CR110" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR110">110</a></sup>, <em>Shewanella</em><sup><a href="#CR111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR111">111</a></sup>, <em>Geobacter</em><sup><a href="#CR112" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR112">112</a></sup>, and <em>Desulfuromonas</em><sup><a href="#CR113" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR113">113</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig2"><h4 class="obj_head">Fig. 2. Microbial fuel cell.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><img class="graphic" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/ad26/10284894/755e13723554/41526_2023_285_Fig2_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d32e1159" height="814" width="632" alt="Fig. 2"></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>MFC consists of two chambers, separated by a proton exchange membrane. In the anode chamber, exoelectrogens, shown as a biofilm in this figure, anaerobically oxidize organic matter releasing protons and electrons. A closer look at this interaction can be seen in the bottom image. The electrons released during the oxidation of organic matter transfer to the anode and travel to the cathode in the second chamber via an external circuit, creating an electrical current. The protons released travel through the proton exchange membrane into the cathode chamber, where the protons and electrons react with oxygen and form water.</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par28">The idea behind MFC has been around for over a century, but it is just within the past few decades that it has become a commercialized product. MFC can produce an energy output up to 5.61 W/m<sup>2</sup><sup><a href="#CR114" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR114">114</a>–<a href="#CR116" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR116">116</a></sup><sup>,</sup> and can also be used for wastewater recycling, toxin removal, bioremediation, and resource recovery<sup><a href="#CR117" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR117">117</a>–<a href="#CR121" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR121">121</a></sup>. These same concepts can be utilized on future Martian or lunar extraterrestrial outposts for energy production and within BLSS. In 2007, de Vet and Rutgers were the first to test the capabilities of MFC energy production under simulated and real microgravity conditions aboard the ISS using <em>Rhodoferax ferrireducens</em> to produce electricity. Energy output averaged 0.1 mA in 1 G, 0.35 mA in simulated microgravity, and 0.02 mA on the ISS. While the differences were not statistically significant, the study did show the potential for MFC to operate in space<sup><a href="#CR122" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR122">122</a></sup>. This mechanism for energy production is not yet practical for space travel due to the low energy output, considering a standard 40 W light bulb draws 0.36 A to operate, but can be initially utilized for its byproducts, such as clean wastewater<sup><a href="#CR123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR123">123</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec8"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Waste recycling as an energy source</h4>
<p id="Par29">Waste can accumulate during space travel in the form of urine, fecal matter, and inedible food and with the help of microbes, this waste can be repurposed for energy production as well as for nutrient recovery and production of potable water. Urine is an excellent feedstock for MFC as it contains high levels of urea, organic ammonium salts, and other organic compounds that microbes can convert into electricity<sup><a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">86</a></sup> thus making urine MFC effective mechanisms for energy production<sup><a href="#CR124" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR124">124</a>–<a href="#CR127" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR127">127</a></sup>. Some urine MFC can not only produce energy but recover nutrients as well. Lu et al. designed a three-chamber MFC to remove organic pollutants, recover N, phosphorus (P), and sulfur (S), and produce energy from urine<sup><a href="#CR125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR125">125</a></sup>. The maximum power output was 1300 mW/m<sup>2</sup>, with almost complete removal of pollutants, including over 97% of urea, total nitrogen, sulfate, phosphate, and chemical oxygen demand, as well as 40% of ammonium, 15% of salts, and 91-99% of organic compounds. The MFC also recovered essential nutrients, including 42% of total N, 37% of phosphate, 59% of sulfate and 33% of total salts<sup><a href="#CR125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR125">125</a></sup>. This recovery technology can be especially valuable within other compartments of BLSS, including plant compartments, by providing nutrient-rich water free of contaminants.</p>
<p id="Par30">In order for urine MFC to also be used as a mechanism to convert urine to potable water, the high level of inorganic salts present in urine (~14.2 g/L)<sup><a href="#CR86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR86">86</a></sup> need to first be removed for MFC to function efficiently<sup><a href="#CR125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR125">125</a></sup>. This can be achieved with an alternative type of MFC, called a microbial desalination cell, which follows the same concept as a standard MFC but with an added desalination chamber between the anode and cathode<sup><a href="#CR128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR128">128</a></sup>. Cao et al. tested this mechanism of water desalination at concentrations comparable to the salinity of urine at 5, 20, and 35 g/L using a mixed bacterial culture, with the salt concentration determined by a change in conductivity of the solution<sup><a href="#CR128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR128">128</a></sup>. This microbial desalination cell produced a maximum power output of 2 W/m<sup>2</sup>, with ~88–94% of salt removed, depending on the initial concentration<sup><a href="#CR128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR128">128</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par31">Other organic components of wastewater, such as human feces, can be a resource for electricity generation by MFC as well. Fangzhou et al. tested the capabilities of MFC to generate electricity from activated sludge obtained from a sewage treatment plant for specific use within BLSS for future crewed outposts<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. Tests were performed using a standard or adjustable two-chamber MFC, a one-chamber MFC with one or two membrane electrode assemblies, and a fermentation pre-treatment device. The highest maximum power output was 70.8 mW/m<sup>2</sup> produced by the two-chamber MFC, however, the authors concluded that for space applications, the one-chamber configuration was better, as it produced a more stable output, at 0.3 V<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. The efficiency of pollutant removal was also tested, with about 44% removal of ammonium and 71% of organic material with each configuration<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. To further increase power generation and toxin removal from fecal wastewater, fermentation pre-treatment was proposed. This involved using reactors filled with anaerobic sludge to degrade fecal macromolecules into smaller organic molecules<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. Pre-treating fecal wastewater by fermentation produced 47% more power than no pre-treatment, suggesting a preference of exoelectrogens within MFC for smaller organic molecules<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>. Based on these results, the authors developed an automatic human feces wastewater MFC system containing a fermentation pre-treatment device to simultaneously dispose of one day’s worth of feces and generate electricity. Indeed, the maximum power output of the system was 240 mW/m<sup>2</sup>, about 3.5-fold higher than the standard two-chamber MFC system<sup><a href="#CR129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR129">129</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par32">Inedible food waste will be an inevitable part of spaceflight and extraterrestrial outposts on the Moon and Mars that need to be disposed of, as on Earth. This organic material can act as substrates in MFC for energy production, Colombo et al. tested the energy producing capabilities of MFC with various food-industry organic wastes as inputs, including those rich in fibers, sugars, proteins, and acid<sup><a href="#CR130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR130">130</a></sup>. A one-chamber MFC was fed each type of organic substrate, and the concentration of organic compounds was measured periodically to obtain the rate of degradation. The maximum power output for each organic waste substrate was 50 mV for sugar, 40 mV for fiber, 30 mV for protein, and 10 mV for acid, with each organic compound degraded by 90%<sup><a href="#CR130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR130">130</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par33">While MFC will be a useful tool to create energy and recycle organic waste beyond LEO, research and development is still ongoing to develop more efficient systems with a larger and sustained power output. Some of these ideas involve the use of different materials (such as ceramics) and configurations (large vs small, stacked vs dispersed)<sup><a href="#CR131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR131">131</a></sup>. Gajda et al. tested a small (70 mm long, 15 mm diameter, 2 mm thickness) and a large (100 mm long, 42 mm diameter, 3 mm thickness) terracotta MFC. They found that the smaller terracotta MFC achieved a power density output 2.9-fold greater than the large MFC, at 20.4 W/m<sup>3</sup> and 7.0 W/m<sup>3</sup>, respectively. Gajda et al. also tested the performance of stacking MFC for a small-scale multi-unit system that could be utilized on future crewed outposts<sup><a href="#CR132" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR132">132</a></sup>. They compared power output of a small module containing 28 MFC units and a larger module containing 560 MFC units. Stacked 560 units created a five-fold improvement in power output of 245 mW compared to the 28 MFC unit. Another concept is the PeePower urinals which collect urine and feces directly from the source, producing energy through multiple ceramic MFC<sup><a href="#CR133" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR133">133</a></sup>. This leads to concentrated wastewater inputted into the MFC rather than diluted samples, which reduces power output. Researchers tested a 288-unit MFC on a university campus which averaged 5–10 users per day. The PeePower urinals were able to produce an average of 75 mW which powered the LED lights directly connected to the MFC stack for 75 h. Another 432-unit MFC was tested during a large music festival which averaged 1000 users per day. In this setting, the PeePower urinals were able to produce an average of 300 mW which successfully powered lighting within the urinals over a seven-day period<sup><a href="#CR133" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR133">133</a></sup>. While the success of PeePower was demonstrated on Earth, it will be important to test similar models of power generation using urine and feces in microgravity. None the less, this research provides the foundation for the development of similar toilet-like MFC to be used for power generation on deep space missions.</p></section><section id="Sec9"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Plant MFC</h4>
<p id="Par34">Plant compartments within BLSS can be used for energy production in MFC as well. Healthy soils contain organic matter from decaying plant litter as well as carbohydrate flux directed out of the roots into the rhizosphere<sup><a href="#CR134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR134">134</a></sup>. In theory, the anode chamber of a MFC could be positioned within the rhizosphere to capitalize on the symbiotic microbes present to oxidize this continuous source of organic matter to generate an electrical current. Such a soil MFC was tested using rice plants, where 330 W/ha of power was produced in the presence of actively growing plants, a seven-fold higher energy output compared to the energy output of soil MFC not using plants<sup><a href="#CR135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR135">135</a></sup>. This technology is not limited to only soil-based systems but can be applied to hydroponic plant systems as well, in which the anode is situated within the water chamber surrounding plant roots<sup><a href="#CR136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR136">136</a></sup>. Research by Lee and Miller, growing <em>Bacopa monnieri</em> and with the addition of <em>Escherichia coli</em>, obtained a power density output of up to 1.9 W/m<sup>2</sup> with a 34% increase in plant growth fueled by plant essential nutrients supplied by <em>E. coli</em> acting within the fuel cell<sup><a href="#CR136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR136">136</a></sup>. In addition to electricity generation, soil MFC can be used for the remediation of heavy metal contaminated regolith<sup><a href="#CR64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR64">64</a></sup>. Habibul et al. tested the ability of soil MFC to remove chromium from soil using ryegrass. The soil MFC was fed a solution of varying concentrations of chromium, resulting in &gt;90% removal efficiency by Proteobacteria and Firmicutes. In addition, the higher the concentration of chromium, the higher the current density output, reaching a maximum of 55 mA/m<sup>2</sup><sup><a href="#CR137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR137">137</a></sup>. These results show the promise of energy generation through plant-system powered MFC with the added benefit of increasing plant yield for consumption by crewmembers.</p></section><section id="Sec10"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Solar power</h4>
<p id="Par35">Photosynthetic microorganisms, such as algae or cyanobacteria, can be utilized to convert light energy into electrical energy, termed microbial electrochemical technology<sup><a href="#CR72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR72">72</a></sup>. Biophotovoltaics is a specific type of electrochemical technology in which phototrophic microorganisms produce electricity by utilizing incoming light energy to split water molecules, generating electrons and protons that can be used to produce an electrical current within an MFC. Several cyanobacteria species have been tested for use in biophotovoltaics, such as <em>Synechocystis</em><sup><a href="#CR138" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR138">138</a>,<a href="#CR139" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR139">139</a></sup><em>, Nostoc</em><sup><a href="#CR140" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR140">140</a>,<a href="#CR141" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR141">141</a></sup><em>, Lyngbya</em><sup><a href="#CR142" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR142">142</a>,<a href="#CR143" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR143">143</a></sup>, and <em>Leptolyngbia</em><sup><a href="#CR144" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR144">144</a>,<a href="#CR145" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR145">145</a></sup>. Kaushik et al. tested the energy producing capabilities of <em>Synechococcus</em> using a two-chamber photosynthetic MFC built with light transparent glass<sup><a href="#CR146" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR146">146</a></sup>. The MFC operated through a 12-h light/12-h dark cycle under a white light intensity of 15 W/m<sup>2</sup>. Maximum power density output of the photosynthetic MFC was 0.61 W/m<sup>2</sup><sup><a href="#CR146" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR146">146</a></sup>. This technology provides a feasible method of energy production on extraterrestrial outposts, but further research needs to be completed to increase power output and optimize light conversion.</p>
<p id="Par36">Research on the use of in situ resources such as wastewater, plant systems, and solar radiation, shows potential for the use of MFC as a mode of power generation and sustainability on extraterrestrial outposts. Though power generation is limited from these substrates at the moment future work may enhance their efficiency. In addition, other sources of power, such as nuclear power, could supplement these MFC systems to provide adequate power generation in habitats and spacecraft beyond LEO<sup><a href="#CR147" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR147">147</a></sup>.</p></section></section><section id="Sec11"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Biomining</h3>
<p id="Par37">Biomining is an environmentally friendly and affordable alternative to traditional physical-chemical mineral processing methods to extract metals of economic interest from rock ores or mine waste. The process involves specific microorganisms that secrete organic acids and metal-binding compounds that essentially dissolve these metals, allowing them to be easily extracted from the environment<sup><a href="#CR148" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR148">148</a></sup>. Biomining is commonly applied to pyritic ores and completed by iron-oxidizing bacteria, such as <em>Thiobacillus ferrooxidans</em><sup><a href="#CR149" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR149">149</a></sup>, <em>Leptospirillum ferrooxidans</em><sup><a href="#CR150" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR150">150</a></sup>, and <em>Acidimicrobium ferrooxidans</em><sup><a href="#CR151" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR151">151</a></sup>. With the reduced iron in the form of pyrite, the bacteria produce iron that oxidizes metal sulfides to sulfuric acid which further accelerates rock dissolution<sup><a href="#CR152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR152">152</a>–<a href="#CR154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR154">154</a></sup>. These species, along with those in the <em>Sulfobacillus</em> and <em>Acidianus</em> genera, as well as many iron-oxidizing bacteria, are used for the biomining of copper, zinc, uranium, nickel, aluminum, and cobalt<sup><a href="#CR155" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR155">155</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par38">The biomining process is not limited to Earth. It may serve as an innovative method for reducing the cost of raw materials and energy requirements beyond LEO, enhancing the sustainability of life on extraterrestrial outposts. Martian and lunar basalt are known to contain many valuable metals, such as iron, nickel, copper, vanadium, and many others, that are suitable substrates that can be biomined by microbes<sup><a href="#CR156" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR156">156</a>,<a href="#CR157" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR157">157</a></sup>. Biomining of these metals from Martian and lunar surfaces could provide the necessary materials for the in-situ construction of buildings, electrical systems, spacecraft equipment, solar cells, and heating and lighting systems in human habitats beyond LEO<sup><a href="#CR158" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR158">158</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par39">Recent research on the ISS simulating biomining of essential compounds from basalt under microgravity demonstrated the possibility for microbial mining beyond Earth<sup><a href="#CR159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR159">159</a>–<a href="#CR161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR161">161</a></sup>. Cockell et al. tested the rare Earth element (REE) biomining capabilities of three microorganisms, <em>Sphingomonas desiccabilis, Bacillus subtilis, and Cupriavidus metallidurans</em>, under three different levels of gravity: microgravity, simulated Martian gravity, and terrestrial gravity, and against a non-biological control<sup><a href="#CR160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR160">160</a></sup>. Biomining reactions took place within biomining reactors. Within each reactor, researchers placed growth media, sterilized basalt slides with a known REE and single strain cultures of each microorganism. Biomining capabilities were assessed based on absolute quantities of REE in ng obtained from 6 mL bulk fluid collected from the biomining reactors and compared to the non-biological control, consisting of a sterile basalt slide without cell inoculation<sup><a href="#CR160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR160">160</a></sup>. REEs assessed include lanthanum, cerium, praseodymium, neodymium, samarium, europium, gadolinium, terbium, dysprosium, holmium, erbium, thulium, ytterbium, and lutetium. The concentration of each REE extracted was proportional to the known abundance in the basaltic rock. At all simulated gravity levels, <em>S. desiccabilis</em> demonstrated enhanced biomining capabilities per gram of basalt substrate, producing 32.52 ng under microgravity, 43.09 ng under Mars gravity, and 32.26 ng under Earth’s gravity, compared to the non-biological mining control, which produced 24.67 ng under microgravity, 21.36 ng under Mars gravity, and 13.25 ng under Earth’s gravity. These values represent the combined mass of biomined REEs. <em>B. subtilis</em> and <em>C. metallidurans</em> demonstrated no differences under the simulated gravity conditions tested and underperformed compared to the non-biological control. As part of the same flight experiment, Cockell et al. tested the biomining capabilities for vanadium (a critical, high-strength element used as a building material), using the same methods and organisms as the Cockell et al. study described above<sup><a href="#CR160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR160">160</a>,<a href="#CR161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR161">161</a></sup>. <em>S. desiccabilis</em> and <em>B. subtilis</em> increased mined vanadium yield, achieving a two-fold increase in mined vanadium 184.92% and 283.22% under microgravity, 216.32% and 219.78% under Mars gravity, and 208.70% and 221.59% under Earth’s gravity, respectively, compared to the control<sup><a href="#CR160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR160">160</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par40">With the abundance of iron in Mars regolith (17.9% wt), iron may be a crucial resource produced through biomining<sup><a href="#CR162" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR162">162</a></sup>. Iron is one of the most-processed metals on Earth that is incorporated in most building materials and would be heavily relied on for construction, repair, and maintenance of buildings at extraterrestrial outposts. Copper is another important metal that can be produced through biomining, with nearly 20–30% of all copper produced on Earth extracted through biomining<sup><a href="#CR162" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR162">162</a></sup>. For over 30 years, copper has been an essential metal used in the construction of rocket engines<sup><a href="#CR163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR163">163</a>,<a href="#CR164" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR164">164</a></sup> and being able to extract copper and other minerals from in situ resources on extraterrestrial outposts will allow engine maintenance and repair to occur beyond LEO, reducing the cost and time of sending replacement parts from Earth.</p>
<p id="Par41">Other economically essential elements have been found in asteroidal material and Martian regolith and can be extracted through biomining<sup><a href="#CR165" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR165">165</a>–<a href="#CR167" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR167">167</a></sup>. These include those in the platinum group, including palladium and osmium, and the 17 REEs. During the Viking Mission to Mars, palladium-silver tubing was utilized in gas chromatography-mass spectrometry to detect organic compounds, and it would be important for future research on Mars in the search for extraterrestrial life<sup><a href="#CR168" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR168">168</a></sup>. In addition to machinery, REE can be used in building and fixing methods for power generation, specifically solar panels<sup><a href="#CR169" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR169">169</a></sup>. Lastly, REE are found in electronic screens and fluorescent lights, both necessary for data collection, communication, and the general well-being of those on extraterrestrial outposts<sup><a href="#CR170" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR170">170</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec12"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">The biomining process</h4>
<p id="Par42">Bioreactors are necessary for biomining reactions to occur. Terrestrial biomining processes most often occur in open, non-sterile tank reactors that require constant stirring to distribute oxygen and nutrients<sup><a href="#CR171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR171">171</a></sup>. To implement biomining on extraterrestrial outposts, it is essential to assess the extent to which differing gravity levels impact microbe-mineral interactions within these stirred-tank bioreactors. An experiment called BioRock, aimed to do this by creating a prototype biomining reactor for space experimentation on the ISS<sup><a href="#CR159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR159">159</a></sup>. The biomining reactor has three main components: the culture chamber, the medium reservoir chamber, and a fixative reservoir chamber, where a fixative is injected to halt microbial growth after the biomining reactions take place (Fig. <a href="#Fig3" class="usa-link">3</a>). Two biomining reactors are placed together within two levels of containment. Pre-test flights found the bioreactors to be successful at growing the model microorganisms, <em>S. desiccabilis, B. subtilis</em>, and <em>C. metallidurans</em>. These microorganisms were chosen as they are low-risk pathogens with the ability to survive desiccation for space flight, limited requirements for growth, and are present in mineral-rich environments. Growth was determined based on optical density in nutrient solution after three weeks. For <em>S. desiccabilis</em>, growth occurred in all tested geometries of biomining reactors, ranging from 0.308 to 0.804 OD<sup><a href="#CR159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR159">159</a></sup>. BioRock has also been successfully used to test REE and vanadium biomining capabilities of <em>S. desiccabilis, B. subtilis</em>, and <em>C. metallidurans</em> in microgravity, Mars gravity and Earth’s gravity<sup><a href="#CR160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR160">160</a>,<a href="#CR161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR161">161</a></sup>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig3"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 3. Schematic depiction of a biomining reactor.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><img class="graphic" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/ad26/10284894/70ad888d1900/41526_2023_285_Fig3_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d32e1619" height="912" width="609" alt="Fig. 3"></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>The biomining reactor has three main components: the culture chamber, the media reservoir chamber, and a fixative reservoir chamber (shown in section 1). The culture chamber is where the biomining reactions take place and where the microorganisms reside before the media is injected. The media reservoir chamber contains the nutrients required for the biomining reaction to occur and is injected into the culture chamber to begin the biomining reaction (section 2). Once the biomining reaction is completed, a fixative is injected to halt microbial growth (section 3).</p></figcaption></figure><p id="Par43">An additional method of biomining, proposed by Volger et al., utilizes a two-bioreactor system and aims to further enhance ISRU on Martian outposts compared to traditional bioreactors<sup><a href="#CR172" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR172">172</a></sup>. The first system is an algae bioreactor, which utilizes <em>Chlorella vulgaris</em> to produce biomass for the biomining reactor and oxygen. The algal biomass is then utilized by <em>Shewanella oneidensis</em> as a growth medium in the biomining reactor. In the biomining reactor, <em>S. oneidensis</em> mines iron ores from Martian regolith; the biomass-rich material left over after extraction can then be used for plant growth. Based on modeled algae growth and biomining performance, the system is projected to produce 0.031 kg O<sub>2</sub> per day and 100 kg of iron per Mars year<sup><a href="#CR172" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR172">172</a></sup>. This model needs to be further tested and future experiments should include exposure to various gravity conditions to assess the impact that this spaceflight stressor will have on growth and performance.</p>
<p id="Par44">The BioRock experiment and other biomining endeavors using iron-oxidizing and alternative candidate bacteria demonstrate potential for biomining in differing gravity levels and the potential for biomining as a source of ISRU at future Martian and lunar outposts. Biomining for elements known to be located within Martian and lunar regolith, such as iron and REE, will be essential for proper maintenance and production of devices and technology that promote sustainability and provide a foundation by which to launch operations for deep space exploration.</p></section></section></section><section id="Sec13"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Bioengineered microbes for space</h2>
<p id="Par45">Microorganisms are an important, renewable resource that can be leveraged to produce pharmaceuticals or therapeutics, biological life support systems, and manufacturing materials for human space exploration and colonization that could help reduce the need for costly resupply missions beyond LEO<sup><a href="#CR173" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR173">173</a></sup>. Candidate microbes can be chosen for these applications based on the availability of genetic tools for manipulation, desired metabolic properties, and tolerance to environmental conditions. These microbes can be further engineered to make them more well-suited for biotechnological applications for interplanetary travel or extraterrestrial settlements using synthetic biology tools. Synthetic biology involves the rational design or repurposing of living organisms and biological systems. Using synthetic biology, microorganisms can be engineered or built de novo with characterized parts and tools to endow them with new or improved functions<sup><a href="#CR173" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR173">173</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec14"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Biotherapeutics</h3>
<p id="Par46">The risk that long-duration space missions pose for crewmembers is not yet completely understood but the extreme conditions, such as microgravity, radiation, and confinement, coupled with microbiome dysregulation may lead to or enhance the disruption of bodily functions<sup><a href="#CR174" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR174">174</a></sup>. Researchers have studied the effect of simulated or actual spaceflight conditions on gastrointestinal (GI) problems<sup><a href="#CR175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR175">175</a></sup>, the development of diseases such as cancer and cardiovascular disorders<sup><a href="#CR176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR176">176</a></sup>, or a predisposition to contracting infections<sup><a href="#CR177" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR177">177</a>–<a href="#CR179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR179">179</a></sup>. The use of probiotics as a countermeasure to combat changes in the microbiome as a result of spaceflight is being investigated to support astronaut health on long-duration space missions<sup><a href="#CR180" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR180">180</a>,<a href="#CR181" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR181">181</a></sup>. Probiotics are living organisms able to survive in the gastric environment that provide health benefits and maintain or improve microbiome balance when consumed. On Earth, probiotics have been used to treat many ailments including weight and muscle loss, inflammation, dermatitis, immune disorders, mental health, and GI conditions (i.e., diarrhea, irritable bowel syndrome (IBS), inflammatory bowel disease (IBD))<sup><a href="#CR180" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR180">180</a>,<a href="#CR182" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR182">182</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec15"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Probiotic viability in space</h4>
<p id="Par47">Promising probiotic candidates for space missions could include <em>Bifidobacterium</em> and <em>Lactobacillus</em>, to counteract their decrease in relative abundance in the astronaut microbiome during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR183" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR183">183</a>,<a href="#CR184" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR184">184</a></sup>. While these species are commercially used on Earth their efficacy and long-term viability when used and stored in space has to still be verified. In 2017, Shao et al. examined the viability of <em>Lactobacillus acidophilus</em> in simulated microgravity and observed no effect on cellular morphology or adhesion. However, some biological changes were present compared to controls, such as increased growth rate at early time points, acid tolerance (pH &lt; 2.5) by ~22–32%, bile tolerance at low concentrations, antibacterial activity, and resistance to antibiotics (i.e., cefalexin, gentamicin, penicillin)<sup><a href="#CR185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR185">185</a></sup>. The following year, the shelf life of freeze-dried <em>Lactobacillus casei</em> strain Shirota press-through capsules was tested in spaceflight conditions aboard the ISS<sup><a href="#CR186" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR186">186</a></sup>. After one month of storage in ambient conditions (i.e., temperature 20–24.5 °C, absorbed dose rate 0.26 mGy/day) and six months after the start of the experiment, bacteria in flight samples were sustained in sufficient numbers that were comparable to ground controls. There were no observed changes in probiotic viability, and the basic probiotic properties of the bacteria including growth rate, carbohydrate fermentation, cell-wall polysaccharide integrity, and resistance to intracellular digestion remained intact upon thawing<sup><a href="#CR186" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR186">186</a></sup>. A lengthier shelf-life analysis of freeze-dried cells for three commercial probiotics including <em>Bifidobacterium longum, L. acidophilus</em> and spores of <em>B. subtilis</em> was then performed in a simulated three-year round-trip to Mars<sup><a href="#CR187" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR187">187</a></sup>. In under 200 days, <em>B. longum</em> and <em>L. acidophilus</em> viability was decreased by about 2-logs while <em>B. subtilis</em> maintained viability up to the end of the experiment (545 days). Therefore, researchers concluded that freeze-dried bacterial spores showed the most promise for withstanding long-duration space missions including ambient spacecraft conditions and radiation with an estimated shelf-life of 4.7 years<sup><a href="#CR187" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR187">187</a></sup>. Overall, these studies provide foundational information on the storage, stability, and viability of probiotic candidates when flown in space. These results suggest that with further testing, probiotic bacteria can be an essential component of the astronaut medical toolkit for the maintenance of a healthy gut microbiome, prevention and treatment of bacterial infections or medical concerns that may arise in future space missions.</p></section><section id="Sec16"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Engineered probiotics to combat infection</h4>
<p id="Par48">In addition to the observed decrease in beneficial bacteria, microbial tracking studies have shown that spaceflight conditions can also lead to an increase in opportunistic pathogens in both the built microbiome and astronaut microbiome. This is particularly problematic due to the dysregulated immunity of astronauts in space<sup><a href="#CR178" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR178">178</a></sup>. Since antibiotics are the most commonly used therapeutic for the treatment of bacterial infections, researchers are investigating whether microbes can be used for antibiotic production in space. This is particularly relevant considering that antibiotics are known to have accelerated degradation and decreased efficacy when flown and stored in space for long periods of time<sup><a href="#CR188" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR188">188</a></sup>. On the Space Shuttle Mission STS-77, Lam et al. analyzed the effects of spaceflight on the production of monorden by <em>Humicola fuscoatra</em> WC5157, a marine fungus. Monorden has demonstrated antimicrobial activity against pathogenic fungi and antitumour activity on human tumor cell growth in vitro<sup><a href="#CR189" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR189">189</a></sup>. Using solid-state fermentation, researchers observed up to 190% increased yield of the antifungal in spaceflight compared to ground controls at 23.8 and 8.2 μg, respectively<sup><a href="#CR190" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR190">190</a></sup>. Similar results were obtained in another study analyzing the production of actinomycin D, an antibiotic and antitumor agent, by <em>Streptomyces plicatus</em> on the ISS.
