After 17 days in orbit, the amount of antibiotic produced by ISS samples increased by up to 577% compared to ground controls<sup><a href="#CR191" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR191">191</a></sup>, and over a 72-day period, researchers noted increased production of actinomycin D in ISS samples specifically at early time points<sup><a href="#CR192" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR192">192</a></sup>. While much work is yet to be done toward on-demand microbial production of antibiotics in space, these studies demonstrate that microbes are a promising platform for this application. Elucidating the mechanism driving this increased yield early in antibiotic production in microgravity could enable the engineering of bacteria for enhanced antibiotic or therapeutic bioprocessing in space or even be applied to increase antibiotic production on Earth.</p>
<p id="Par49">Antibiotic-associated diarrhea (AAD) may arise in space as a side effect of administering antibiotics to treat infections. Several studies have investigated the use of <em>Debaryomyces hansenii</em> as a treatment for AAD in a mouse model. <em>D. hansenii</em> is commonly used in the food industry for the processing of cheese and has been identified as part of the human gut microbiome<sup><a href="#CR193" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR193">193</a>,<a href="#CR194" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR194">194</a></sup>. When administered for treatment of AAD, <em>D. hansenii</em> alters the composition of the microbiome by promoting the growth of beneficial lactase-producing bacteria and by inhibiting the growth of opportunistic pathogens<sup><a href="#CR195" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR195">195</a>–<a href="#CR197" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR197">197</a></sup>. In one study, the presence of Proteobacteria in the intestinal mucosa increased in response to diarrhea, from ~19% in a normal group to ~36% in an AAD model group, and treatment with <em>D. hansenii</em> was able to restore Proteobacteria to normal levels<sup><a href="#CR195" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR195">195</a></sup>. Proteobacteria abundance often positively correlates with IBD and inflammation and is generally regarded as an indicator of microbiome instability<sup><a href="#CR198" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR198">198</a></sup>. In spaceflight, the proportion of Proteobacteria in the astronaut skin microbiome was decreased, namely Gammaproteobacteria and Betaproteobacteria<sup><a href="#CR175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR175">175</a></sup>, while it was increased in the salivary microbiome<sup><a href="#CR199" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR199">199</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par50">While bacterial infections are typically treated with antimicrobials, the formation of biofilms and propagation of multi-drug resistance in the spaceflight environment (discussed later in this review) limits these therapeutic options. A promising alternative currently being investigated on Earth is the use of engineered microorganisms as live biotherapeutics (e.g., biosensors, probiotics with enhanced benefits, and drug delivery systems)<sup><a href="#CR200" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR200">200</a>–<a href="#CR204" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR204">204</a></sup>. Well-established microbial chassis organisms include <em>E. coli</em> and <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em>, though a microbe more well-suited to the target therapeutic environment or application can also be chosen. One example using a less conventional chassis was recently performed by Garrido et al. where they engineered <em>Mycoplasma pneumoniae</em>, a human lung pathogen, as a live biotherapeutic to treat <em>S. aureus</em> and other biofilm-associated infections in vivo<sup><a href="#CR205" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR205">205</a></sup>. Researchers created an attenuated strain of <em>M. pneumoniae</em> able to secrete anti-biofilm and bactericidal enzymes, dispersin B and lysostaphin, and demonstrated its ability to eliminate an <em>S. aureus</em> biofilm in a mouse model<sup><a href="#CR205" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR205">205</a></sup>. Another candidate chassis that could be employed to eradicate pathogens is <em>D. hansenii</em>, which produces volatile organic compounds and mycocins that have demonstrated antimicrobial effects on several pathogenic bacteria and fungi. An alternative to secreting bactericidal proteins to kill pathogens is the incorporation of CRISPR/Cas9 gene-editing technology into synthetic designs to create engineered probiotics for targeted bacterial killing. This was demonstrated by Neil et al. by delivering CRISPR/Cas9 on a conjugative plasmid which led to 99.9% eradication of antibiotic resistant <em>E. coli</em> and complete eradication of <em>Citrobacter rodentium</em> in the GI tract of a mouse model<sup><a href="#CR206" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR206">206</a></sup>. CRISPR/Cas9 gene editing has also been demonstrated in <em>D. hansenii</em><sup><a href="#CR207" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR207">207</a></sup>, and could be applied to engineer this strain for increased production of mycocins that can target <em>C. albicans</em><sup><a href="#CR208" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR208">208</a>,<a href="#CR209" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR209">209</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par51">Microbes can also be engineered as biosensors to identify or inhibit pathogenic bacteria by sensing an important indicator molecule and releasing a signal or enzyme in response. For instance, biological targeting systems have been demonstrated using engineered <em>E. coli</em> for directed killing of the biofilm-associated pathogen <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em>, a bacterium that has been demonstrated to have increased biofilm formation and pathogenicity on the ISS<sup><a href="#CR210" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR210">210</a></sup>. Saeidi et al. engineered <em>E. coli</em> with a synthetic genetic circuit containing three modules: sensing, lysing, and killing. The sensing module includes a constitutively expressed transcription factor, <em>lasR</em>, which detects and binds to <em>N</em>-Acyl homoserine lactone (AHL), a quorum sensing molecule released from <em>P. aeruginosa</em>. This bound complex activates the lysing and killing modules expressing lysis E7 and pyocin S5 proteins, respectively. This leads to perforation of the <em>E. coli</em> cell membrane and release of the bacteriocin which targets the pathogen and killed 99% of viable cells<sup><a href="#CR211" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR211">211</a></sup>. Following this study, Hwang et al. programmed <em>E. coli</em> with a modular circuit containing the same sensing module coupled to a motility and killing module. The motility module expressed the chemotaxis protein CheZ to initiate motility toward the pathogen, while the killing module produced antimicrobial and biofilm-degrading proteins, MccS and DNaseI. Viability of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> was examined and found that <em>E. coli</em> harboring both the motility and killing biosensor modules resulted in the killing of 60% of cells<sup><a href="#CR210" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR210">210</a></sup>. Biological sensors to detect and/or reduce pathogenic bacteria using traditional chassis such as engineered <em>Lactobacillus</em> or <em>E. coli</em> have also been demonstrated against intestinal <em>P. aeruginosa</em><sup><a href="#CR212" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR212">212</a></sup>, vancomycin-resistant <em>Enterococcus</em><sup><a href="#CR213" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR213">213</a></sup><em>, Candida albicans</em><sup><a href="#CR214" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR214">214</a></sup>, and <em>S. aureus</em><sup><a href="#CR215" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR215">215</a>,<a href="#CR216" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR216">216</a></sup>. These studies highlight the vast potential of engineered microbes to sense and kill space microbiome-associated pathogens and disrupt biofilms. Since the choice of probiotics is both bacteria- and ailment-dependent<sup><a href="#CR217" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR217">217</a></sup>, the use of synthetic biology to create genetically engineered biotherapeutics with higher complexity and multiple functions (i.e., able to target multiple pathogens) is vital to minimize the amount of cargo on future space missions. The viability of these therapeutics for humans and their ability to function in the conditions of spaceflight still need to be investigated, but these examples help to shed light on what the next generation of engineered biotherapeutics could offer.</p></section><section id="Sec17"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Engineered probiotics for disease prevention and detection</h4>
<p id="Par52">Engineered probiotics can also be a valuable tool for the prevention or detection of more serious health issues such as GI disorders, kidney stones, cancer, and cardiovascular disease (CVD), or the treatment of their associated symptoms. Space-induced changes in the gut microbiome observed in astronauts aboard the ISS by Voorhies et al. included an increase in <em>Parasutterella</em>, a bacteria associated with IBD<sup><a href="#CR175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR175">175</a></sup>. In IBD pathology, purinergic receptors are activated by extracellular adenosine triphosphate (eATP) released by commensal gut bacteria and immune cells, promoting intestinal inflammation. Engineered yeast probiotics containing a human P2Y2 purinergic receptor have been developed for the treatment of IBD by responding to physiological eATP levels and secreting the eATP-degrading enzyme apyrase<sup><a href="#CR218" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR218">218</a></sup>. This probiotic was shown to be effective in a mouse model of IBD, decreasing intestinal inflammation and dysbiosis. Bacterial probiotics can also be engineered to detect gut inflammation by sensing tetrathionate, thiosulfate or nitric oxide<sup><a href="#CR219" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR219">219</a>,<a href="#CR220" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR220">220</a></sup>, or with programmable memory systems to detect and respond to an environmental stimulus<sup><a href="#CR221" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR221">221</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par53">The risk of kidney stones due to bone decalcification, dehydration, or increased growth rate of calcium-depositing nanobacteria<sup><a href="#CR222" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR222">222</a>–<a href="#CR224" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR224">224</a></sup> is increased in spaceflight, which could also be prevented or treated using probiotics. Calcium oxalate is the major component of kidney stones, therefore ideal probiotic bacteria are efficient in oxalate degradation, such as <em>Oxalobacter formigenes</em><sup><a href="#CR225" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR225">225</a></sup> and <em>B. subtilis</em>. <em>B. subtilis</em> strain 168 has been presented as a novel probiotic therapy as it has been shown to break down the oxalate in kidney stones in a <em>Drosophila</em> model<sup><a href="#CR226" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR226">226</a></sup>. The oxalate decarboxylase (OxDC) enzyme derived from <em>B. subtilis</em> can also be used to engineer other bacteria as probiotics for the treatment of kidney stones. The expression and subsequent purification of this enzyme in <em>E. coli</em> was able to reduce oxalate concentrations in a mouse model in urine and feces by 44% and 72%, respectively, compared to controls<sup><a href="#CR227" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR227">227</a></sup>. The OxDC gene was also introduced into <em>Lactobacillus plantarum</em> on a plasmid, leading to expression and secretion of this enzyme where it was able to degrade 70–77% of oxalate in vitro, and reduced oxalate as well as calcium, uric acid, creatinine, serum uric acid, and BUN/creatinine ratio in urine compared to controls in a rat model<sup><a href="#CR228" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR228">228</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par54">Probiotics including <em>Lactobacillus</em> and <em>Bifidobacterium</em> can also be used to improve cardiovascular health by reducing weight, cholesterol, and adipose tissue while also preventing or attenuating injuries to the heart (e.g., heart failure, ischemia, cardiac hypertrophy)<sup><a href="#CR229" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR229">229</a></sup>. Using a rat model, <em>Lactobacillus rhamnosus</em> or a placebo was administered to subjects following coronary artery occlusion for a six-week duration. Compared to placebo controls, rats given the probiotic treatment showed attenuation of left ventricular hypertrophy, improved systolic and diastolic left ventricular function, and additional improvements up to six weeks after withdrawing treatment<sup><a href="#CR230" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR230">230</a></sup>. Other <em>Lactobacillus</em> species have had positive effects on CVD including <em>L. plantarum</em> which helped to reduce serum levels of leptin and fibrinogen, which are CVD risk factors<sup><a href="#CR231" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR231">231</a></sup>. These probiotics can also be genetically engineered to enhance their potential benefits. For instance, <em>E. coli</em> Nissle 1917 has been genetically engineered to produce N-acylphosphatidylethanolamines which, when administered to mice, led to decreased adiposity, insulin resistance and lipid accumulation in the liver<sup><a href="#CR232" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR232">232</a></sup>. This has important implications for astronauts as spaceflight can induce negative, aging-like effects on the cardiovascular system (i.e., decreased fitness, arterial stiffening, and insulin resistance) and radiation exposure has been well-characterized to increase the risk of developing radiation-induced cardiovascular disease (RICVD)<sup><a href="#CR233" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR233">233</a>,<a href="#CR234" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR234">234</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par55">Crewmembers are at an elevated risk of cancer development due to radiation and other spaceflight factors<sup><a href="#CR235" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR235">235</a>–<a href="#CR237" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR237">237</a></sup>. As such, methods for cancer prevention and treatment are important to implement during deep space exploration and bacterial-mediated cancer therapies could be a promising approach Bacteria can be used naturally or engineered for cancer therapy to specifically target and colonize tumors, or as a drug delivery system for anticancer agents<sup><a href="#CR238" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR238">238</a></sup>. Many bacteria have been investigated for these applications including <em>Bifidobacterium</em><sup><a href="#CR239" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR239">239</a></sup>, <em>E. coli</em><sup><a href="#CR240" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR240">240</a>–<a href="#CR242" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR242">242</a></sup>, <em>Clostridium</em><sup><a href="#CR243" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR243">243</a>,<a href="#CR244" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR244">244</a></sup>, <em>Salmonella</em><sup><a href="#CR245" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR245">245</a>–<a href="#CR249" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR249">249</a></sup>, and <em>Streptococcus</em><sup><a href="#CR250" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR250">250</a></sup> species. A study using <em>E. coli</em> engineered with synthetic adhesins to target a tumor antigen in vivo found that lower doses of engineered <em>E. coli</em> were required to colonize tumors compared to wild-type controls<sup><a href="#CR240" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR240">240</a></sup>. Tumor targeting was also demonstrated in an attenuated strain of <em>Salmonella typhimurium</em> harboring a short hairpin RNA expression plasmid. It was engineered to target inhibin, a tumor marker, resulting in significant inhibition of colon cancer and melanoma growth in a mouse tumor model<sup><a href="#CR247" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR247">247</a></sup>. Bacteria can also be engineered to improve tumor and metastasis visualization within mammalian hosts, facilitating their use as diagnostic and therapeutic microbial agents. For example, <em>E. coli</em> was engineered to express an acoustic reporter gene allowing them to be imaged noninvasively in vivo and to produce protein-nanoparticle gas vesicles for targeted breast cancer therapy<sup><a href="#CR242" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR242">242</a></sup>. <em>E. coli</em> was also engineered with <em>lacZ</em>, encoding the <em>β-galactosidase</em> reporter, which can be easily detected in urine as an indicator of liver metastasis<sup><a href="#CR251" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR251">251</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par56">Bacteria hold great potential for the development of easily modifiable biotherapeutics that could be invaluable for treatment or prevention of health issues during long-duration space missions. However, additional research and clinical validation is necessary before employing engineered bacteria as biotherapeutics for space-associated disorders and diseases.</p></section></section><section id="Sec18"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Life support and nutrition</h3>
<p id="Par57">BLSS can provide crew members with oxygen, food, and water, and will be imperative for long-duration space missions and for the establishment of sustainable human habitats on the Moon or Mars. Due to their diverse applications for spaceflight, microalgae and cyanobacteria are often studied for their incorporation in BLSS and photobioreactors. They produce oxygen, remove carbon dioxide from the environment and help with water purification<sup><a href="#CR104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR104">104</a>,<a href="#CR105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR105">105</a>,<a href="#CR252" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR252">252</a>,<a href="#CR253" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR253">253</a></sup>. These microbes are also edible allowing their biomass to provide nutritional and therapeutic benefits without the need for protein purification<sup><a href="#CR35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR35">35</a>,<a href="#CR254" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR254">254</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec19"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Enhanced photosynthesis</h4>
<p id="Par58">Oxygen for astronauts on the ISS is currently transported in pressurized tanks from Earth or is produced using water onboard through electrolysis<sup><a href="#CR255" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR255">255</a></sup>. Therefore, the ability to improve carbon uptake and oxygen output using microbes on the ISS, for space travel and in future extraterrestrial habitats, is an essential step toward the creation of sustainable and self-sufficient systems. Oxygen production, CO<sub>2</sub> capture, and photosynthetic capacity could be enhanced in BLSS using synthetic biology tools to address the bottleneck of photosynthesis: the carbon fixation cycle. Metabolic engineering of cyanobacteria can improve photosynthetic capacity as demonstrated by Berepiki et al. where expression of mammalian cytochrome P450 (CYP1A1) acting as an electron sink in <em>Synechococcus</em> PCC 7002 improved photosynthetic efficiency and increased electron flow rate by ~30%<sup><a href="#CR256" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR256">256</a></sup>. Using the same cytochrome P450 gene, Santos-Marino et al. engineered metabolic pathways for sucrose production and cytochrome P450 as a carbon and electron sink, respectively, into <em>Synechococcus elongatus</em>. Ultimately, this resulted in increased photosynthesis, and simultaneous expression of both sinks had an additive effect on photosystem I oxidation and photosystem II efficiency<sup><a href="#CR257" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR257">257</a></sup>. Another strategy to improve this cycle is by increasing the substrate concentration of ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase-oxygenase (RuBisCo) to improve carbon uptake. In the cyanobacterium <em>Synechocystis</em> sp. PCC6803, one study found that genetic installation of additional bicarbonate transporters resulted in a 2-fold increase in carbon uptake and biomass<sup><a href="#CR258" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR258">258</a></sup>. Metabolic engineering of cyanobacteria can also be used to produce industrially relevant high-value chemicals and bioproducts such as biofuels. Some engineering efforts have been demonstrated in the model cyanobacteria species, <em>S. elongatus</em> PCC7942 and <em>Synechocystis</em> sp. PCC6803, to produce ethanol, ethylene, isobutyraldehyde, and isoprene<sup><a href="#CR259" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR259">259</a>–<a href="#CR262" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR262">262</a></sup>. The biotechnologically relevant bacteria, <em>Ralstonia eutropha</em> (i.e., <em>C. necator</em>) has also been engineered by Dogutan and Nocera to capture CO<sub>2</sub> to produce biofuels and edible biomass, in an artificial photosynthetic cycle that is much more efficient than those that are naturally occurring<sup><a href="#CR263" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR263">263</a></sup>.</p></section><section id="Sec20"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Microbial production of nutrients</h4>
<p id="Par59">In addition to oxygen, microalgae can provide a sufficient source of proteins, carbohydrates, fatty acids, minerals, and vitamins required for a balanced diet<sup><a href="#CR264" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR264">264</a>,<a href="#CR265" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR265">265</a></sup>. These nutritional outputs can be further enhanced using synthetic biology approaches. Genome editing technologies for these marine species have expanded over the last 20 years, namely due to improvements in DNA sequencing, manipulation techniques and availability of genomic information<sup><a href="#CR266" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR266">266</a></sup>. Some of these methods and technologies include DNA delivery via conjugation, the generation of auxotrophic strains, and DNA-free or plasmid-based genome editing (e.g., using CRISPR/Cas9)<sup><a href="#CR267" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR267">267</a>–<a href="#CR270" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR270">270</a></sup>. Therefore, it is possible to create cell factories using metabolic engineering to alter the composition or nutritional output of these species<sup><a href="#CR271" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR271">271</a>–<a href="#CR273" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR273">273</a></sup>. For example, mutagenesis and CRISPR/Cas9 gene editing technologies have been used to modify the biomass composition of the model algae species <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em>. Irradiated mutant strains have been generated with double the starch content compared to the wild-type strain through increased expression of phosphoglucomutase 1 (PGM1) and decreased expression of downstream enzymes in the glycolytic pathway<sup><a href="#CR274" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR274">274</a></sup>. <em>C. reinhardtii</em> has also been engineered to knock out the zeaxanthin epoxidase gene resulting in 47-fold increased production of the carotenoid zeaxanthin, which is important in the prevention of macular degeneration<sup><a href="#CR275" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR275">275</a></sup>, a concern facing astronauts during prolonged spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR276" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR276">276</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par60">Synthetic biology approaches are also being used to genetically engineer microorganisms for the production and long-term storage of nutrients as part of NASA’s BioNutrients projects<sup><a href="#CR277" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR277">277</a></sup>. Nutrient production and storage on long-duration space missions is a challenge as they can degrade over time. Therefore, this project aims to develop a system for on-demand microbial production of micronutrients on the ISS, whereby packages of dehydrated, edible yeast can be hydrated and consumed. In the first segment of the project, BioNutrients-1, <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> and <em>S. boulardii</em> species were engineered to produce antioxidants with genes for zeaxanthin and beta-carotene biosynthesis pathways, respectively<sup><a href="#CR277" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR277">277</a></sup>. The <em>S. boulardii</em> genome was also engineered to stimulate increased trehalose stores and with tardigrade-derived cytosolic abundant heat soluble (CAHS) genes, both resulting in increased tolerance to desiccation. Along with these strains, several other edible microorganisms are being tested for their storage and survival in stasis packages in the ambient conditions of the space environment. These include yogurt-producing and milk-coagulating bacteria (<em>Lactobacillus delbrueckii subsp. bulgaricus, Streptococcus salivarius subsp. thermophilus, B. subtilis</em> and <em>Bacillus coagulans</em>), yeasts (<em>Kluyveromyces lactis</em> and <em>Komagataella phaffii Kurtzman</em>) and C1-utilizing bacteria (<em>Methylobacterium extorquens</em> and <em>C. necator)</em>. The production and stasis packages were delivered to the ISS and will be analyzed for growth and nutrient expression for a five-year duration. After 47 days, initial stasis package data showed no significant difference in the viability of bacteria stored on the ISS compared to ground controls<sup><a href="#CR278" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR278">278</a></sup>. These organisms are all attractive candidates for biological engineering to produce vitamins, therapeutics, or other useful enzymes for maintaining crew health. In addition to continued testing of the production of carotenoids, BioNutrients-2<sup><a href="#CR279" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR279">279</a></sup> aims to further develop the bioproduction system from BioNutrients-1 by expanding the variety of probiotic products on the ISS to include yogurt and kefir and investigate the production of follistatin by the engineered yeast <em>K. lactis</em><sup><a href="#CR279" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR279">279</a></sup>. The results of the BioNutrients project will provide invaluable information for the feasibility of using microbes as a platform for nutrient storage and production for long-term space travel.</p></section></section><section id="Sec21"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Engineered biomaterials</h3>
<p id="Par61">In-space manufacturing and development can be challenging as necessary materials and supplies are not readily available and currently need to be transported or resupplied from Earth. Microbes offer a solution as they can be reprogrammed for the production of biologically derived materials (i.e., bioplastics, nanomaterials)<sup><a href="#CR280" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR280">280</a></sup> to generate useful components such as plastics, adhesives, composites, and rubbers for structural space applications.</p>
<section id="Sec22"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Gel-based materials</h4>
<p id="Par62">One material that would be beneficial to produce in situ beyond LEO is aerogels. Aerogels, first created by Kistler in 1931, are human-made, low-density solid materials with an interconnected porous network composed of 99.8% air, with the most common type being silica-based<sup><a href="#CR281" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR281">281</a></sup>. Photosynthetic organisms that can produce silica, primarily algae diatoms (<em>e.g., Phaeodactylum tricornutum</em>), are of interest for the generation of silica-based aerogels and have recently been incorporated into cellulose aerogel composites<sup><a href="#CR282" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR282">282</a>,<a href="#CR283" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR283">283</a></sup>. Aerogels are useful materials for space applications due to their low thermal conductivity, light weight, and high porosity<sup><a href="#CR284" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR284">284</a>–<a href="#CR286" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR286">286</a></sup>. To improve the habitability of other planets for humans and photosynthetic organisms, silica aerogels provide the benefit of allowing for the transmission of visible light for photosynthesis while simultaneously blocking hazardous UV radiation<sup><a href="#CR284" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR284">284</a></sup>. In addition, since aerogels are a thermally insulating material, they can raise surface temperatures through the solid-state greenhouse effect<sup><a href="#CR284" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR284">284</a></sup>. NASA is currently taking advantage of the thermal insulating properties of aerogels to protect spacecraft and rovers from the cold Martian surface temperature, such as for the Mars Pathfinder lander, Mars exploration rovers (Spirit and Opportunity) and Mars Science Laboratory mission (Curiosity rover)<sup><a href="#CR286" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR286">286</a></sup>. Aerogels have also been used as a method for capturing particles from space without damaging them, as demonstrated in the Stardust Mission<sup><a href="#CR286" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR286">286</a></sup>. Therefore, using a synthetic biology approach to generate aerogels from silica-producing organisms could be interesting to investigate further.</p>
<p id="Par63">Other gel-based materials can also be synthesized using synthetic biology. For instance, Kim et al. took advantage of the structural properties of synthetic spider silk<sup><a href="#CR287" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR287">287</a>,<a href="#CR288" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR288">288</a></sup> and mussel adhesive proteins, which can act as biological adhesives<sup><a href="#CR289" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR289">289</a>,<a href="#CR290" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR290">290</a></sup>, to engineer <em>E. coli</em> with a spidroin-amyloid-mussel foot hybrid protein that can ultimately assemble into a hydrogel with high strength and underwater adhesion<sup><a href="#CR291" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR291">291</a></sup>. The hybrid protein consisted of a zipper domain from an amyloid protein, a flexible domain from spider silk, and a dihydroxyphenylalanine (DOPA)-containing mussel foot protein. This is a great example of how synthetic biology can be used to generate bacterial hosts expressing recombinant proteins with novel or desirable functions and properties, which can be applied to tackle specific manufacturing challenges in space. In addition, methods to spatially control the distribution of microbial cells into hydrogel structures have been developed using a Stereolithographic Apparatus for Microbial (SLAM) Bioprinting 3D printer<sup><a href="#CR292" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR292">292</a></sup>. Bioresins composed of synthetic polymers were used to contain the microorganisms and mimic extracellular polymeric substances (EPS) that are fundamental to biofilm formation in nature<sup><a href="#CR293" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR293">293</a></sup>. The power of this technology was demonstrated by printing engineered <em>Caulobacter crescentus</em> as uranium biosensors within this biomaterial using a uranium responsive promoter fused to GFP and measuring the fluorescence output<sup><a href="#CR292" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR292">292</a></sup>. This technology could enable genetic engineering of single strains, microbiomes, and biofilms to be used not only for biomanufacturing but also for biomining, biotherapeutics, and bioremediation.</p></section><section id="Sec23"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Polymer production and degradation</h4>
<p id="Par64">Plastic materials continue to play a vital role in the manufacturing of spacesuits and spacecraft. Progress has been made towards the sustainable production of high-strength, biodegradable plastics in engineered microorganisms. Specifically, efforts have been made to increase the production of polyhydroxyalkanoate (PHA) or polyhydroxybutyrate (PHB) in engineered cyanobacteria, <em>Synechococcus</em><sup><a href="#CR294" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR294">294</a></sup> and <em>Synechocystis</em> sp. PCC6803<sup><a href="#CR295" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR295">295</a>,<a href="#CR296" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR296">296</a></sup>. <em>Synechocystis</em> sp. PCC6803 has been engineered with the PHA biosynthetic pathway of <em>R. eutropha</em> (i.e., Cupriavidus necator)<sup><a href="#CR296" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR296">296</a></sup> or the overexpression of <em>sigE</em><sup><a href="#CR295" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR295">295</a></sup> to increase PHA and PHB production, respectively. Metabolic engineering of the shikimate pathway in <em>B. subtilis</em> or <em>S. cerevisiae</em> for the increased production of <em>para</em>-aminobenzoic acid (pABA) has also been studied, which can act as a precursor for high-strength polymers (e.g., aramid fibers)<sup><a href="#CR297" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR297">297</a>,<a href="#CR298" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR298">298</a></sup>. Ultimately, these biologically derived materials can be used to manufacture parts or even 3D-print hardware in space<sup><a href="#CR299" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR299">299</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par65">To make in-space manufacturing and construction off-planet more sustainable, ISRU or recycling of existing material components to produce feedstock for new materials is necessary<sup><a href="#CR300" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR300">300</a></sup>. Microorganisms offer a solution to this problem as many can naturally degrade polymers for metabolic products. Black fungi, which are a diverse group of extremophilic melanized fungi, have been investigated for this application due to their demonstrated ability to hydrolyze synthetic polymers<sup><a href="#CR301" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR301">301</a></sup>). For instance, <em>Aureobasidium pullulans</em>, a black fungus, has demonstrated microbial deterioration of plasticized polyvinyl chloride (PVC) and dioctyl adipate plasticizers<sup><a href="#CR302" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR302">302</a></sup>. <em>Knufia chersonesos</em>, another black fungus, has been shown to completely break down the synthetic copolymer polybutylene adipate terephthalate (PBAT)<sup><a href="#CR303" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR303">303</a></sup>. Secretome screening identified seven polyesterase enzymes that could potentially be involved in this observed polymer degradation, which lays the foundation for the possibility of further engineering of this biosynthetic pathway for more efficient degradation<sup><a href="#CR303" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR303">303</a></sup>. Therefore, this group of extremophilic organisms are promising candidates for plastic degradation, however, this process still needs to be investigated in simulated or spaceflight microgravity.</p>
<p id="Par66">Microgravity studies of <em>K. cheronesos</em> that analyzed the effects of simulated microgravity on the proteome and secretome found that scytalone dehydratase gene expression was upregulated in the wild-type strain and downregulated in a melanin-deficient mutant strain<sup><a href="#CR304" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR304">304</a></sup>. This enzyme is involved in the biosynthesis of dihydroxynaphthalene melanin, which is believed to have protective qualities that fungi use to withstand the extreme conditions of space<sup><a href="#CR305" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR305">305</a></sup>. This suggests that <em>Knufia</em> species, and potentially other black fungi have the properties to withstand space conditions and are good candidates for plastic degradation in space. Since then, various other extremophilic fungi have been tested for their survival in space, through exposure to simulated Mars conditions<sup><a href="#CR306" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR306">306</a>,<a href="#CR307" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR307">307</a></sup>. These include 12 Chernobyl-isolated strains (i.e., <em>Cladosporium</em>, <em>Acremonium</em>, <em>Beauveria</em>, <em>Fusarium</em>, <em>Trichoderma</em>, <em>Penicillium</em>, <em>Aureobasidium</em>, <em>Aspergillus</em> and <em>Apiospora</em>), the black fungi <em>Exophiala jeanselmei</em>, and the microcolonial fungi <em>Cryomyces antarcticus</em> and <em>Knufia perforans</em>. Plastic degradation has also been investigated in extremophilic bacteria, for example <em>Streptomyces thermoviolaceus</em>, <em>Geobacillus thermocatenulatus</em> and <em>Clostridium thermocellum</em><sup><a href="#CR308" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR308">308</a></sup>. As these extremophilic fungi and bacteria have been shown to withstand space conditions, thus they are promising candidates to use or engineer for plastic degradation in space. Ultimately, the capacity for manufacturing in space will be beneficial for long-duration space missions, reducing the need to bring materials as cargo or have them launched from Earth, and allowing for on-demand production of materials based on immediate need.</p></section></section><section id="Sec24"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Myco-architecture</h3>
<p id="Par67">Astronauts venturing out beyond Earth’s protective magnetosphere will be exposed to hazardous radiation during deep-space exploration missions. This includes high-energy electromagnetic waves from our sun such as UV radiation, gamma, and X-rays or sub-atomic particles from the cosmos (electrons, protons, neutrons, and heavy metal ions), known as galactic cosmic radiation. These forms of radiation strip electrons from molecules resulting in protein or DNA damage through production of reactive oxygen and nitrogen species<sup><a href="#CR309" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR309">309</a>,<a href="#CR310" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR310">310</a></sup>. The result is short-term or long-term health problems such as cancer, acute radiation sickness, radiation-induced cardiovascular disease, and neurological damage<sup><a href="#CR278" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR278">278</a></sup>. Developing a solution for passive radiation protection for astronauts will be a critical step towards sustaining long-term presence on the Moon and Mars. The average person on Earth is exposed to about 6.2 mSv of radiation over a period of a year, while the average astronaut on the ISS is exposed to approximately 144 mSv<sup><a href="#CR311" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR311">311</a></sup>. One year into a three-year mission to Mars, an astronaut would already have been exposed to some 400 mSv of radiation<sup><a href="#CR311" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR311">311</a>,<a href="#CR312" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR312">312</a></sup>. Due to the complex nature of space radiation, there is likely no one-size-fits-all solution to this problem. Some proposed architectural concepts for radiation production include building below ground in lava tubes or piling meters of regolith outside of a structure<sup><a href="#CR313" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR313">313</a>,<a href="#CR314" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR314">314</a></sup> and while materials like lead and aluminum may be effective, they would be costly to transport<sup><a href="#CR315" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR315">315</a></sup>. As a result, the search for innovative radiation shields will depend in part on biotechnology, which holds unique advantages such as suitability for ISRU, self-regeneration, and adaptability. By selecting model organisms such as extremophiles, that use radiation as an energy source, we can begin to understand their properties and refine testing for technology development.</p>
<p id="Par68">Fungi on Earth have been isolated in high-radiation environments, such as the contamination zone of the Chernobyl Nuclear Power Plant<sup><a href="#CR316" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR316">316</a>,<a href="#CR317" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR317">317</a></sup>, inside the ISS<sup><a href="#CR317" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR317">317</a></sup>, and exteriors of spacecraft in LEO<sup><a href="#CR305" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR305">305</a></sup>. Analogous to phototrophy, fungi appear to perform radiosynthesis, using pigments known as melanin to convert gamma radiation into chemical energy<sup><a href="#CR318" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR318">318</a>,<a href="#CR319" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR319">319</a></sup>. Melanin has the capability to absorb electromagnetic radiation, resist acids, and perform powerful antioxidant activity allowing some fungi to thrive in the most extreme environments on Earth or beyond, including those with high levels of ionizing radiation<sup><a href="#CR320" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR320">320</a>,<a href="#CR321" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR321">321</a></sup>. Studies examining the survival rates of melanized and non-melanized yeasts with gamma radiation have shown that melanin-rich fungi were able to shield ionizing radiation at efficacies comparable to lead and twice as effective as charcoal, whereas the non-melanized strains lacked the capability to provide shielding<sup><a href="#CR322" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR322">322</a></sup>. This may not only be due to the presence of melanin itself but the spatial arrangement of it within the cell, as it was observed that in <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em>, melanin arranged in a spherical shape, covering the inner surface of the cell membrane, resulted in superior shielding from radiation<sup><a href="#CR323" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR323">323</a></sup>, hypothesized to be due to the increase in scattering of incident photons<sup><a href="#CR318" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR318">318</a></sup>. Melanin is not the sole mechanism by which fungi survive radiation exposure. In a study using melanized yeast <em>Exophiala dermatitidis</em>, it was found that nutrient availability, culture density, metabolic state and DNA repair mechanisms were better determinants of cell survival after gamma radiation exposure than melanin<sup><a href="#CR324" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR324">324</a></sup>. Other mechanisms that protect fungi against radiation involve enzymes that remove reactive oxygen species or those that promote DNA repair, either through nucleotide excision or photoreactivation<sup><a href="#CR325" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR325">325</a></sup>, and various other secondary metabolites, such as the antioxidant pyranonigrin A<sup><a href="#CR326" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR326">326</a></sup>. Due to the ability of various fungi to withstand, thrive, and even attenuate space-relevant doses of radiation<sup><a href="#CR306" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR306">306</a>,<a href="#CR316" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR316">316</a>,<a href="#CR318" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR318">318</a>,<a href="#CR327" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR327">327</a>–<a href="#CR330" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR330">330</a></sup> there is keen interest in their use for the development of radiation-resistant shields or structures<sup><a href="#CR305" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR305">305</a>,<a href="#CR330" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR330">330</a>–<a href="#CR332" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR332">332</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par69">Fungal mycelium, a filamentous network of hyphae, is a fibrous material that can be used as structural components for the construction of habitats, buildings, furniture, etc.<sup><a href="#CR333" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR333">333</a></sup>. Fungal mycelium as a construction material has attractive characteristics including self-healing potential, impressive compressive strength, flexibility, insulation, and hydrophobicity<sup><a href="#CR333" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR333">333</a></sup>. The use of mycelium-based materials and structures in space would be a sustainable, biodegradable option with demonstrated uses for generating textiles<sup><a href="#CR332" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR332">332</a></sup> and as an alternative to plastic packaging. Haneef et al. used two edible and medicinal fungi, <em>Ganoderma lucidum</em> and <em>Pleurotus ostreatus</em>, to produce mycelium films composed of polysaccharides, lipids, protein, and chitin<sup><a href="#CR334" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR334">334</a></sup>. The fungi were grown on two different substrates; cellulose or cellulose with PDB, and the final composition and characteristics of the film differed based on which substrate they were exposed to<sup><a href="#CR334" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR334">334</a></sup>. This suggests that myco-architecture properties could be modulated simply by varying the growth substrate. Biocomposites combining fungal mycelium with cellulose plant fibers<sup><a href="#CR335" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR335">335</a></sup> or with wood and cellulose nanofibrils have also been investigated<sup><a href="#CR336" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR336">336</a></sup>. Since they are living organisms, synthetic biology could be used to engineer fungi to secrete other useful structural components such as polymers, for even more complex structures. Indeed, CRISPR-Cas9 methodology has been used to create gene deletions in <em>Paecilomyces variotii</em>, a Chernobyl fungal isolate, and was used to identify the gene responsible for its radiation resistance<sup><a href="#CR337" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR337">337</a></sup>. This technology could be further employed to enhance various fungi for deep space applications, such as making them better suited for radiation shielding.</p></section><section id="Sec25"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Concrete production</h3>
<p id="Par70">Another promising construction material to shield humans, plants and (possibly even) animals from the harsh Lunar and Martian environments is concrete. Concrete is a promising material for space applications as it is strong (it has proven to be the most durable material against natural disasters and extreme weather events), resistant to burns, rust and rot and could be made with engineered microbes and in situ resources. Concrete is composed of three main components: water, cement, and an aggregate (i.e., sand, gravel). Researchers have discovered that the use of human serum albumin combined with regolith from the Moon or Mars as the concrete aggregate can produce a concrete-like biocomposite that is made even stronger with the addition of urea<sup><a href="#CR338" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR338">338</a></sup>. Alternatives for cement, one of the main components of concrete, can be made using engineered bacteria to express recombinant or structural proteins, such as bovine or human serum albumin<sup><a href="#CR339" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR339">339</a></sup> and spider silk<sup><a href="#CR340" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR340">340</a></sup>. This way, protein production and purification can be scaled-up and ultimately mixed with in situ regolith, rather than extracting them directly from the source.</p>
<p id="Par71">Microbes can further aid in the production of concrete through soil biocementation using microbially induced calcite precipitation (MICP)<sup><a href="#CR341" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR341">341</a></sup>. This is a process wherein microorganisms precipitate calcium carbonate, the main component of limestone and cement, by urea hydrolysis or CO<sub>2</sub>-concentration<sup><a href="#CR341" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR341">341</a></sup>. For this reason, candidate microbes are typically highly urease-active bacteria, such as <em>Sporosarcina pasteurii</em> (formerly known as <em>Bacillus pasteurii</em>)<sup><a href="#CR342" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR342">342</a>,<a href="#CR343" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR343">343</a></sup>. One of the limitations of concrete is that it can often crack due to shrinkage. Incorporation of microorganisms into these structures can provide a sustainable solution for in-space construction but also a long-term biotechnological solution for concrete maintenance through crack remediation. Bacteria embedded in the concrete, resulting in what is often termed “living” or “self-healing” concrete, can repair cracks that form over time through the precipitation of calcium carbonate crystals<sup><a href="#CR344" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR344">344</a></sup>. This has been investigated using microorganisms including microalgae (<em>Synechococcus</em> and <em>Spirulina</em>)<sup><a href="#CR345" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR345">345</a>,<a href="#CR346" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR346">346</a></sup>, <em>Bacillus</em><sup><a href="#CR347" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR347">347</a></sup>, and <em>Deinococcus radiodurans</em><sup><a href="#CR348" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR348">348</a></sup>. Concrete is a harsh, alkaline environment (~pH 12) and therefore relies on hardy microbes tolerant of these conditions. The ability for <em>Bacillus</em> to form spores and its resistance to alkaline conditions makes this microbe a promising candidate for survival in the high pH concrete environment. Concrete remediation using MICP by spore-forming <em>Bacillus</em> species was demonstrated by Ramachandran et al.<sup><a href="#CR349" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR349">349</a></sup> and later by Jonkers et al., who demonstrated that the spores remained viable for up to four months<sup><a href="#CR350" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR350">350</a></sup>. While this is a promising start, experiments need to be performed to determine whether bacterial spores can remain viable for longer durations. The <em>B. subtilis</em> gene cluster and molecular mechanism involved in calcium carbonate biomineralization have been identified<sup><a href="#CR351" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR351">351</a></sup> and therefore could be used to engineer novel bacteria with enhanced self-healing capabilities or resistance to the concrete environment.</p>
<p id="Par72">Using extremophilic bacteria that can tolerate both the concrete and space environment to produce calcium carbonate may allow for the construction of Lunar or Martian structures with increased durability or radiation resistance. One such candidate is the cyanobacterium <em>Synechococcus</em>, a photosynthetic organism that can withstand extreme environmental conditions. Using <em>Synechococcus</em> PCC8806, Zhu et al. incorporated cyanobacteria into a mixture of hydrated concrete powder and sand. Results showed that 38% more calcium was precipitated in conditions containing cyanobacteria than in controls, and the microbial calcite layer on the concrete surface was thicker, more adhesive, and decreased water absorption by twofold<sup><a href="#CR345" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR345">345</a></sup>. More recently, <em>Synechococcus</em> sp. PCC 7002, was tested for incorporation in a sand-hydrogel scaffold<sup><a href="#CR352" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR352">352</a>,<a href="#CR353" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR353">353</a></sup>. Following inoculation into a scaffold of sand, gelatin and media, the microbial viability was analyzed in varying temperature and humidity conditions. While the scaffold was strongest at ambient conditions, cells were not viable after seven days, however at 50% or 100% humidity, 9% and 14% of the cells remained viable after 30 days, respectively<sup><a href="#CR352" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR352">352</a></sup>. Since desiccation appeared to reduce cell viability, another study analyzed whether changes in component ratios or addition of trehalose would allow for increased viability. They found that using a desiccation protectant like trehalose led to increased cell viability at ambient temperature without sacrificing the mechanical properties of the structure<sup><a href="#CR353" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR353">353</a></sup>. Maintaining cell viability of cyanobacteria in the concrete structure is vital for enhancing the crack-healing potential, which was shown to be promising in a recent study using <em>S. elongatus</em> and <em>S. platensis</em> at varying concentrations (i.e., 4, 8 and 12%)<sup><a href="#CR346" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR346">346</a></sup>. An alternative strategy to combat desiccation would be the use of a desiccation-resistant organism such as <em>D. radiodurans</em>, which is a polyextremophilic bacteria known for its resistance to radiation. Unlike some of the other candidate bacteria, <em>D. radiodurans</em> is non-spore forming and therefore has applications for low-temperature conditions<sup><a href="#CR348" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR348">348</a></sup>. <em>D. radiodurans</em> was shown to be viable for up to 28 days in concrete, providing significant crack healing and increasing the compressive strength of the mortar by 42% at room temperature and 38% at near-freezing temperatures<sup><a href="#CR348" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR348">348</a></sup>. Strategies enlisting microbes in combination with Lunar or Martian regolith provide a blueprint for ISRU for manufacturing strong, radiation-resistant structures that could further space exploration efforts and the possibility for human habitation on the Moon and Mars.</p></section></section><section id="Sec26"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Negative impact of microbes and mitigation strategies</h2>
<p id="Par73">While microbes offer many benefits and biotechnological solutions for extended spaceflight and the development of sustainable habitats beyond LEO, they can also have a negative impact on space exploration. This can manifest as increased microbial pathogenicity, horizontal gene transfer (HGT) of problematic genes, and biofilm formation which have direct effects on crew health and spacecraft integrity. Understanding how microbes respond to the space environment, will allow for mitigation strategies to be developed and implemented, to ensure successful long-duration space exploration.</p>
<section id="Sec27"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Pathogenicity</h3>
<p id="Par74">Several studies have demonstrated that the conditions and stress of spaceflight can enhance microbial virulence or pathogenicity, as has been observed with many human-associated pathogens either grown or isolated on the ISS and/or in simulated microgravity conditions. Examples include <em>Klebsiella pneumonia</em><sup><a href="#CR354" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR354">354</a></sup>, <em>P. aeruginosa</em><sup><a href="#CR355" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR355">355</a></sup>, <em>Salmonella enterica</em> serovar Typhimurium<sup><a href="#CR356" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR356">356</a>,<a href="#CR357" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR357">357</a></sup>, <em>Serratia marcescens</em><sup><a href="#CR358" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR358">358</a></sup>, enterotoxigenic <em>E. coli</em><sup><a href="#CR359" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR359">359</a></sup>, and <em>Aspergillus fumigatus</em><sup><a href="#CR360" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR360">360</a></sup>. The presence of microorganisms with heightened virulence could increase the risk of crewmembers contracting infections, even more problematic due dysregulated immunity during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR179">179</a></sup>. <em>S. typhimurium</em> grown under modeled microgravity (MMG)<sup><a href="#CR356" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR356">356</a></sup> and in spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR357" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR357">357</a></sup> showed increased virulence compared to ground controls as demonstrated by an increased mortality rate in a murine infection model. Similarly, <em>S. marcescens</em><sup><a href="#CR358" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR358">358</a></sup> and the pathogenic fungi, <em>Aspergillus fumigatus</em><sup><a href="#CR360" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR360">360</a></sup>, grown on the ISS or in simulated microgravity exhibited enhanced virulence compared to terrestrial strains and were more lethal in a <em>Drosophila</em> and zebrafish model, respectively. The specific mechanism responsible for increased microbial virulence in space conditions is still under investigation but genes with altered expression in-flight may contribute to this virulence response. These include biofilm-associated genes which were found to be upregulated in <em>S. typhimurium</em> (e.g., <em>wca/wza</em> genes for colanic acid synthesis, <em>ompA</em>, <em>fimH</em>) and bacterial motility genes which were downregulated<sup><a href="#CR361" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR361">361</a></sup>. In addition, transcriptional, and proteomic analysis of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> identified genes that were upregulated in spaceflight including virulence- and adhesin-associated lectin genes (<em>lecA</em> and <em>lecB</em>) and <em>rhlA</em> which is involved in biosurfactant production<sup><a href="#CR355" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR355">355</a></sup>. However, the most common factor contributing to space-induced virulence seems to be transcriptional regulation by Hfq, the expression of which was shown to be decreased under both low shear modeled microgravity (LSMMG) and spaceflight conditions in <em>S. typhimurium</em><sup><a href="#CR361" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR361">361</a>,<a href="#CR362" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR362">362</a></sup> and <em>P. aeruginosa</em><sup><a href="#CR355" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR355">355</a>,<a href="#CR363" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR363">363</a></sup>. Hfq is an RNA chaperone and global transcriptional regulator responsible for controlling the expression of a large array of genes and is necessary for the virulence of several bacterial pathogens<sup><a href="#CR355" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR355">355</a></sup>. Consistent with previous studies, <em>hfq</em> was found to be downregulated in <em>S. aureus</em> grown in LSMMG, however, unlike previous studies the virulence potential was reduced<sup><a href="#CR364" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR364">364</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par75">Conversely, some studies have concluded that while microorganisms with pathogenic potential are present in these space environments, spaceflight conditions do not lead to increased virulence or microbial characteristics that would directly impact crew health<sup><a href="#CR365" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR365">365</a>–<a href="#CR367" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR367">367</a></sup>. For example, using a macrophage infection assay, O’Rourke et al. found that there was no significant difference in the virulence of <em>Burkholderia</em> species isolated from the ISS compared to Earth controls<sup><a href="#CR368" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR368">368</a></sup>. In addition, investigation of four common clinical pathogens, <em>Listeria monocytogenes</em>, methicillin-resistant <em>S. aureus</em>, <em>Enterococcus faecalis</em>, and <em>C. albicans</em> revealed that they were less virulent in space regarding their ability to kill <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em> nematodes<sup><a href="#CR369" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR369">369</a></sup>. Similar conclusions were drawn in the ISS experiment EXTREMOPHILES, where sequencing analysis and physiological tests were performed on microbial communities from several surfaces aboard the spacecraft<sup><a href="#CR370" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR370">370</a></sup>. Researchers proposed that while the ISS environment selects for bacteria with more natural resistance and tolerance to extreme conditions, it does not induce genetic or phenotypic changes that result in more extremophilic, or antibiotic resistant bacteria compared to a built, enclosed environment on Earth<sup><a href="#CR370" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR370">370</a></sup>. Indeed, a pan-genomic analysis of microbes from the ISS and built environments on Earth found that the antimicrobial resistance (AMR) genes present in ISS samples were also present in control samples, and that functional changes common to built environments do not have a direct impact on astronaut health<sup><a href="#CR371" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR371">371</a></sup>. Collectively, these studies demonstrate the impact of spaceflight on individual pathogenic microbes in built environments and, for many, it remains to be determined if the virulence phenotype depicted in their models will directly translate to mixed bacterial populations or lead to enhanced pathogenicity in humans. Therefore, assessing the microbiome within the host and continuing to elucidate the mechanism involved in potential spaceflight-enhanced virulence will be necessary. Although there is some debate regarding the increased pathogenicity of microbial populations in space, there is evidence of indirect health concerns caused by harmful biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR370" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR370">370</a></sup>.</p></section><section id="Sec28"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Biofilms</h3>
<p id="Par76">Microorganisms in the space environment, as on Earth, can exist in a planktonic (freely suspended) state, but more commonly form robust biofilms as a tactic for growth and survival. A biofilm is an assemblage of surface-associated microbial cells surrounded by an extracellular matrix of polysaccharides, extracellular DNA (eDNA), proteins, lipids, and other components, with a defined architecture<sup><a href="#CR372" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR372">372</a>,<a href="#CR373" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR373">373</a></sup>. Biofilm-associated organisms differ from their planktonic counterparts with respect to the genes that are transcribed, proteins that are translated and growth rate<sup><a href="#CR372" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR372">372</a></sup>. Although most bacterial biofilms are harmless, some threaten human health and safety and can be difficult to eradicate due to increased resistance to the immune system’s defenses, UV radiation, extreme temperatures, pH, high salinity, high pressure, limited nutrients, and various antimicrobials<sup><a href="#CR374" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR374">374</a>,<a href="#CR375" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR375">375</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par77">Environmental stressors are known to induce biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR374" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR374">374</a></sup> and spaceflight is one such stressor. The impact of microgravity on biofilm formation was investigated for the first-time using <em>P. aeruginosa</em><sup><a href="#CR376" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR376">376</a></sup>, and later during two NASA-funded studies, Micro-2 and Micro-2A<sup><a href="#CR377" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR377">377</a></sup>. In these NASA studies, P. <em>aeruginosa</em> grown on the ISS displayed different biofilm characteristics compared to ground controls such as an increased number of viable cells, biomass, and thickness and a novel column-and-canopy shaped architecture<sup><a href="#CR377" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR377">377</a></sup>. However, this novel architecture was only observed in biofilms formed by motile bacteria, as non-motile strains produced flat structures similar to those seen with the ground controls<sup><a href="#CR377" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR377">377</a></sup>. In another early biofilm investigation experiment conducted in space, <em>Burkholderia cepacia was</em> grown in sterile water, tryptic soy broth (TSB), and an iodine solution (a disinfectant), for six days on stainless-steel coupons<sup><a href="#CR378" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR378">378</a></sup>. Results showed that bacteria grown in space, in sterile water, had a biofilm plate count (measured as CFU/m<sup>2</sup>) five times larger compared to ground controls, however, the space grown TSB population was one quarter of that on Earth<sup><a href="#CR378" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR378">378</a></sup>. Those grown in the iodine solution in space, also had a higher biofilm growth compared to the ground controls, suggesting increased resistance to disinfectants during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR378" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR378">378</a></sup>. In addition, spaceflight conditions have been demonstrated to upregulate gene expression for the production of extracellular matrix proteins leading to enhanced cell aggregation compared to ground controls in <em>C. albicans</em><sup><a href="#CR361" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR361">361</a></sup>. Differential expression of genes related to motility, which is important for the formation of biofilms, has also been observed in flight conditions<sup><a href="#CR379" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR379">379</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec29"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Biofilms and biodeterioration</h4>
<p id="Par78">Microbially influenced corrosion (MIC), refers to the deterioration of metals and nonmetallic materials due to microbial activity, most often due to biofilms. As biofilms increase in quantity more microbial-surface reactions occur, enhancing structural and/or functional damage, causing accelerated biocorrosion<sup><a href="#CR380" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR380">380</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par79">In a recent Microbial Tracking study (MT-1) of the ISS, the bacterial bioburden quantified from various surfaces was as high as 10<sup>9</sup> CFU/m<sup>2</sup><sup><a href="#CR381" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR381">381</a></sup>. Some of the biofilm-forming microorganisms that were identified, <em>Methylobacterium, Sphingomonas, Bacillus, Penicillium</em>, and <em>Aspergillus</em> have been implicated in MIC on Earth<sup><a href="#CR382" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR382">382</a>–<a href="#CR385" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR385">385</a></sup> with <em>Bacillus polymira</em>, <em>Penicillium rubens</em> and <em>Aspergillus</em> sp. responsible for progressive destruction of a navigation window on board Mir<sup><a href="#CR386" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR386">386</a></sup>. <em>Sphingomonas sp</em>. and <em>Methylobacterium sp</em>. have not only been detected on surfaces but also in portable drinking water on the ISS<sup><a href="#CR387" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR387">387</a></sup>. Over the course of 15 years (from its launch in 1986 to 2001), 234 species of bacteria and fungi were identified onboard the MIR space station, with many exhibiting potential polymer biodegradation properties<sup><a href="#CR388" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR388">388</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par80">Biofilm growth has been observed in the Soviet/Russian (Salyuts and Mir), American (Skylab), and International (ISS) Space Stations. Aboard spacecraft, biofilms can jeopardize vital equipment and threaten astronaut health by corroding surfaces or clogging life-support systems including air and water purification systems, spacesuits, navigation windows and radiators<sup><a href="#CR368" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR368">368</a>,<a href="#CR389" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR389">389</a>–<a href="#CR392" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR392">392</a></sup>. Most notable on the ISS, is the microbial contamination and biofilm formation that occurs in the wastewater tank of the Water Recovery System (WRS), which is a part of the Environmental Control and Life Support System (ECLSS) and used to process wastewater from various sources (i.e., urine, cabin condensate) into potable water for crew and other functions<sup><a href="#CR393" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR393">393</a></sup>. For future planned missions beyond LEO to the Moon and Mars, resupplying spare parts or support materials to repair the listed spaceflight systems would be impractical as missions could be in the order of years, in the case of a Mars missions, thus various strategies to control biofilms, especially in critical life support systems are essential<sup><a href="#CR394" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR394">394</a></sup>. Ways to detect, monitor and control biofilms are being explored, such as the current spaceflight BAC (Bacterial Adhesion and Corrosion) study. The aim of this study is to identify bacterial genes relevant to biofilm growth in space, examine whether the formed biofilms corrode stainless steel surfaces mimicking those in the ISS water system, and determine whether silver-based disinfectants can prevent or control extensive biofilm formation.</p></section><section id="Sec30"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Biofilms and astronaut health</h4>
<p id="Par81">Biofilm formation is an important characteristic in the infectious disease process of microorganisms. It has been demonstrated that bacteria can genetically and physically modify their tolerances to LEO conditions, with one such mechanism being biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR395" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR395">395</a>–<a href="#CR397" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR397">397</a></sup>. Human opportunistic pathogens that form or increase biofilms under simulated microgravity conditions or when grown on the ISS include <em>E. coli</em>, <em>S. typhimurium</em>, P. <em>aeruginosa</em> and <em>Micrococcus luteus</em><sup><a href="#CR379" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR379">379</a>,<a href="#CR398" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR398">398</a></sup>. It has also been observed that many species of bacteria and fungi become more antibiotic resistant and pathogenic when exposed or grown in spaceflight conditions<sup><a href="#CR174" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR174">174</a></sup>. In cases of <em>S. typhimurium</em><sup><a href="#CR17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR17">17</a></sup> and <em>P. aeruginosa</em><sup><a href="#CR355" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR355">355</a></sup>, the observed increased virulence (discussed earlier in this review) was attributed to molecular and phenotypic changes consistent with biofilm formation. In the case of <em>Klebsiella pneumoniae</em> grown aboard the Shenzhou VIII spacecraft, the enhanced antibiotic resistance was associated with adaptations related to biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR354" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR354">354</a>,<a href="#CR399" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR399">399</a></sup>. As biofilm formation can increase the risk of human illnesses, through harder-to-treat infections, biofilm properties under space conditions need to be well understood to enable safe, long-duration, human space missions. This is even more imperative considering the immune dysregulation of astronauts and lower efficacy of pharmaceuticals during spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR174" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR174">174</a></sup>.</p></section><section id="Sec31"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Positive impact of biofilms</h4>
<p id="Par82">While biofilms can be detrimental to astronaut health and structural stability, they can be beneficial in areas such as plant protection, bioremediation, wastewater treatment, and corrosion inhibition, amongst others<sup><a href="#CR400" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR400">400</a></sup>. Thus, strategies for manipulating biofilms should not focus on complete eradication, but rather regulation, to promote the growth of beneficial ones while inhibiting the growth of harmful ones<sup><a href="#CR400" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR400">400</a></sup>. These beneficial applications of biofilms may also be extended to spaceflight. Ichikawa et al. developed a long-term life support system that uses an electrochemically activated biofilm reactor<sup><a href="#CR401" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR401">401</a></sup>. This system was tested for the removal of nitrate produced from biological nitrification and is an important process to allow for long-term survival of aquatic organisms in a closed system<sup><a href="#CR401" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR401">401</a></sup>. Results from this biofilm-electrode reactor study showed that neither ammonia nor nitrite accumulated, and nitrate could be suppressed to about 10 ppm<sup><a href="#CR401" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR401">401</a></sup>. Biofilms can also provide insight into how humans tolerate spaceflight. Biological dosimetry is an internationally approved method to perform an exposure assessment following a suspected radiation overexposure. In contrast to physical methods, which measure the actual dose, biological dosimeters measure dose effects, at the cellular level, when assessing the impact of radiation exposure on humans<sup><a href="#CR402" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR402">402</a></sup>. In the BIODOS project, four DNA-based biological dosimeters (phage T7, uracil thin layer, spores, and biofilms) were validated for their effectiveness in determining the biological hazards of environmental UV exposure (i.e., sunlight), and were shown to be reliable field dosimeters<sup><a href="#CR403" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR403">403</a></sup>. This same biofilm dosimeter was then used in a study by Rettberg et al. to determine the biological effectiveness of the UV radiation climate at different locations in the space station, with the aim of ensuring that astronauts had enough UVB to synthesize vitamin D<sup><a href="#CR404" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR404">404</a></sup>. Conclusions from this study showed that the amount of UV radiation inside the station was not sufficient for an adequate supply of vitamin D and that specialized UV lamps were needed to maintain healthy levels of Vitamin D for astronaut health<sup><a href="#CR404" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR404">404</a></sup>. Overall, more work on biofilm applications for spaceflight is needed to tease out the benefits biofilms may offer, while reducing any harmful properties.</p></section></section><section id="Sec32"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Horizontal gene transfer</h3>
<p id="Par83">The ability for bacteria to survive or even thrive in the spaceflight environment, with potentially increased pathogenicity and biofilm formation, may be attributed to DNA transfer. Horizontal gene transfer (HGT), also known as lateral gene transfer, is the movement of genetic material from one organism to another by means other than sexual reproduction or vertical transfer from a parent cell. This typically occurs through one of three main mechanisms: direct contact of microbial cells by a pilus (i.e., conjugation), natural DNA uptake from the environment (i.e., transformation), or introduction by bacteriophages (i.e., transduction)<sup><a href="#CR405" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR405">405</a>–<a href="#CR407" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR407">407</a></sup>. More recently, gene transfer agents and membrane vesicles are being recognized for their contributions to HGT as well<sup><a href="#CR408" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR408">408</a></sup>. HGT is central to microbial evolution because it allows microorganisms to acquire novel genetic material, which may confer a fitness advantage to adapt to or thrive within a specific environment<sup><a href="#CR22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR22">22</a>,<a href="#CR409" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR409">409</a>–<a href="#CR411" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR411">411</a></sup>.</p>
<section id="Sec33"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Prevalence of HGT in the spaceflight environment</h4>
<p id="Par84">In the sealed spaceflight environment, bacteria must adapt to extreme conditions including microgravity and cosmic radiation<sup><a href="#CR412" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR412">412</a></sup>, which could have an impact on the prevalence of genetic transfer. The three main mechanisms of HGT (transduction, transformation, and conjugation) were first investigated for their occurrence in space by Ciferri et al. during the Spacelab D1 mission (STS-61-A, 1986)<sup><a href="#CR413" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR413">413</a></sup>. They discovered no significant difference in transduction, inconclusive transformation results, and increased transmission of antibiotic resistance genes by conjugation in <em>E. coli</em> exposed to microgravity<sup><a href="#CR413" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR413">413</a></sup>. It was postulated that this increase in conjugation frequency could be due to a decrease in mating pair disruptions in microgravity compared to Earth gravity, as continuous cell-to-cell contact is required for conjugation to occur. Another spaceflight experiment was performed on the Discovery Mission (STS-63) where researchers concluded that transformation efficiency to <em>E. coli</em> was decreased in microgravity<sup><a href="#CR414" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR414">414</a></sup>. Later, during the Soyuz Mission 8S on the ISS, the Mobilisatsia/Plasmida experiment examined plasmid-mediated conjugation through triparental mating of both Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria<sup><a href="#CR415" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR415">415</a></sup>. They found that conjugation was increased in experiments between <em>Bacillus thuringiensis</em> strains (Gram-positive), while no trends were observed in conjugation experiments from <em>E. coli</em> to <em>C. metallidurans</em> (Gram-negative)<sup><a href="#CR415" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR415">415</a></sup>. This increase in conjugation efficiency could be attributed to the mechanistic differences between Gram-positive and Gram-negative conjugation, as Gram-positive bacteria facilitate contact between donor and recipient cells through surface adhesins rather than conjugative pili<sup><a href="#CR416" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR416">416</a></sup>. However, authors caution the drawing of conclusions due to failure to obtain transconjugants in some replicates of ground controls. Conversely, conjugation experiments performed using the same Gram-positive species (<em>B. thuringiensis</em>) in simulated microgravity showed that there was no significant difference in plasmid transfer frequency for a plasmid mobilizing itself (<em>cis</em>) or mobilizing a separate plasmid (<em>trans</em>) compared to standard laboratory conditions<sup><a href="#CR417" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR417">417</a></sup>. Most recently, Urbaniak et al. tracked HGT of two antimicrobial resistance (AMR) genes by co-culturing of two species, <em>Acinetobacter pittii</em> and <em>S. aureus</em>, isolated from the ISS as part of the Microbial-1 tracking study<sup><a href="#CR381" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR381">381</a></sup>. The results indicated an approximately 100-fold increase of HGT in simulated microgravity compared to Earth gravity controls (1-<em>g</em>)<sup><a href="#CR418" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR418">418</a></sup>.
