Combined, these results indicate that HGT occurs in spaceflight conditions within and between Gram-negative and Gram-positive bacteria and is typically not hampered, and is more often increased, by spaceflight conditions.</p></section><section id="Sec34"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">HGT and astronaut health</h4>
<p id="Par85">The human gut houses a diverse microbial population with ecologically favorable conditions for HGT given its continuous supply of nutrients and consistent environmental conditions (e.g., temperature, pH)<sup><a href="#CR14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR14">14</a></sup>. Phylogeny-based research suggests that over the course of their evolution, more than half of total genes in the genomes of human-associated microbiota were introduced by HGT<sup><a href="#CR419" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR419">419</a></sup>. While HGT is a normal occurrence within the human microbiome, an increased number of transfer events passing AMR genes from commensal bacteria to opportunistic pathogens during spaceflight could cause changes in these microbial communities<sup><a href="#CR175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR175">175</a></sup>. These transfer events have been investigated in <em>Staphylococcus epidermidis</em>, a bacterium normally present in the human epithelial microbiome, which harbors the methicillin resistance gene <em>mecA</em> on a mobile genetic element. HGT was found to be an important factor for the acquisition of <em>mecA</em> by the pathogen <em>S. aureus</em>, leading to methicillin-resistant <em>S. aureus</em> (MRSA)<sup><a href="#CR420" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR420">420</a>,<a href="#CR421" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR421">421</a></sup>. Recent research has also demonstrated the transfer of AMR and virulence-associated genes from commensal <em>E. coli</em> to pathogenic <em>E. coli</em><sup><a href="#CR422" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR422">422</a></sup>. The transfer of AMR genes can cause increased bacterial resistance and virulence, which could have significant health implications<sup><a href="#CR423" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR423">423</a>,<a href="#CR424" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR424">424</a></sup> for astronauts due to their dysregulated immunity on long-duration space missions<sup><a href="#CR425" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR425">425</a></sup>. Numerous studies have shown that HGT is the common mechanism by which AMR genes are disseminated within an environment, leading to the emergence of multi-drug resistant bacteria<sup><a href="#CR426" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR426">426</a>–<a href="#CR428" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR428">428</a></sup>, which limits treatment options for bacterial infections.</p>
<p id="Par86">Some research has proposed that the increased virulence or pathogenicity observed in some organisms grown under spaceflight conditions is attributed to possible increased HGT in spaceflight conditions<sup><a href="#CR357" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR357">357</a>,<a href="#CR429" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR429">429</a>,<a href="#CR430" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR430">430</a></sup>. In a study comparing similar species from two extreme built environments, one on Earth (Concordia Research Station in Antarctica) and one in space (ISS), it was observed that 76% of the isolates from the ISS were resistant to one or more antibiotics tested compared to only 44% of the Concordia isolates<sup><a href="#CR429" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR429">429</a></sup>. This increased resistance amongst the ISS isolates could be attributed to the higher number of mobile genetic elements (involved in HGT) within their genomes compared to the Antarctic strains<sup><a href="#CR429" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR429">429</a></sup>. The reason for this increase in mobile genetic elements is unclear but it could be a response to the unique stressors of the space environment. Further, in a study published by Urbaniak et al. examining the ISS ‘resistome’, whole genome sequencing revealed AMR gene clusters in <em>Enterobacter bugandensis</em> isolated from the waste and hygiene compartment on the ISS. Further examination showed that these isolates shared AMR gene clusters with known pathogens from different genera which were not present in any <em>Enterobacter</em> species isolated on Earth<sup><a href="#CR430" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR430">430</a></sup>. The researchers proposed that these AMR genes may have been acquired through HGT, but further analysis is necessary to conclude this with certainty. Changes in HGT-associated gene expression were also identified in ISS-derived isolates of <em>S. typhimurium</em> during Space Shuttle mission STS-115, which exhibited enhanced virulence and increased biofilm formation in a mouse model compared to the ground control<sup><a href="#CR357" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR357">357</a></sup>. The expression of <em>hfq</em>, an RNA chaperone and negative regulator of the F plasmid-encoded <em>tra</em> genes, was decreased in spaceflight. <em>TraJ</em> is an activator of the <em>tra</em> operon, and <em>hfq</em> has been shown to specifically repress <em>traJ</em> expression by destabilizing its mRNA<sup><a href="#CR431" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR431">431</a></sup>. As a result, several <em>tra</em> genes were upregulated in response to spaceflight<sup><a href="#CR357" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR357">357</a></sup>. Mating assays performed in an <em>hfq</em> mutant showed an increase in protein levels of TraJ confirmed by immunoblotting as well as increased conjugation efficiency compared to wild-type controls<sup><a href="#CR431" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR431">431</a></sup>. Due to the multiple AMR genes found on the environmental surfaces of the ISS<sup><a href="#CR430" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR430">430</a></sup> and possible enhanced HGT activity during spaceflight, further studies into HGT and the effect on crew health are is important to understand for the development of mitigation strategies<sup><a href="#CR432" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR432">432</a>–<a href="#CR434" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR434">434</a></sup>. In addition, further elucidation of differentially expressed genes and their effect on HGT could identify novel gene targets for modulating or reducing HGT in the space environment.</p></section><section id="Sec35"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">HGT and biofilms</h4>
<p id="Par87">As previously discussed, biofilms can be detrimental to spacecraft and astronaut health; thus, understanding the factors that can influence biofilm formation will be imperative for predicting, preventing, and mitigating spacecraft contamination. HGT may contribute to biofilms as their formation can be induced by conjugative plasmids that express factors to enhance cell-to-cell contact and pilus formation. Conjugative pili can act as adhesion factors as demonstrated by Ghigo who monitored biofilm formation on Pyrex slides submerged in cultures of <em>E. coli</em> K12. They observed that strains carrying a conjugative F plasmid (F+) formed thick biofilms after one day (2 × 10<sup>10</sup> CFU/cm<sup>2</sup>), while plasmid-free strains (F−) only formed microcolonies (8 × 10<sup>5</sup> CFU/cm<sup>2</sup>)<sup><a href="#CR435" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR435">435</a></sup>. Conjugative pili specifically were shown to be a contributing factor to biofilm formation as strains carrying plasmids with mutations in the pilin gene, <em>traA</em>, were unable to form biofilms<sup><a href="#CR435" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR435">435</a></sup>. Reisner et al. confirmed Ghigo’s findings as the presence of the F plasmid pOX38 in <em>E. coli</em> led to the formation of mushroom-shaped biofilms with increased biomass, surface coverage and thickness compared to F- controls<sup><a href="#CR436" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR436">436</a></sup>. To further analyze the contribution of the pilus to this phenotype, the authors created plasmid mutants for several of the genes required for pilus synthesis: <em>traQ</em>, <em>traX</em>, <em>traD</em>, <em>traS</em>, and <em>traT</em><sup><a href="#CR436" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR436">436</a></sup>. Mutants involved in pilus assembly and modification (i.e., <em>traQ, traX</em>) displayed a weak biofilm phenotype similar to the plasmid-free strain (<em>traQ</em>) or decreased biomass and thickness (<em>traX</em>), while those involved in DNA transfer (i.e., <em>traD</em>) displayed rapid confluent growth and tower-like structures<sup><a href="#CR436" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR436">436</a></sup>. In a different study performed with 403 natural <em>E. coli</em> isolates researchers observed biofilm formation in 56 isolates and of those, 89% contained conjugative plasmids<sup><a href="#CR437" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR437">437</a></sup>. These recipients of conjugative plasmids were able to induce biofilms to a greater extent than their plasmid-free controls<sup><a href="#CR437" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR437">437</a></sup>. Finally, one study validated that conjugative plasmids enhanced biofilm formation but concluded that this did not directly correlate with conjugation frequency for all plasmids tested, suggesting other factors may be involved<sup><a href="#CR438" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR438">438</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par88">Other studies suggest that conjugative plasmid-encoded fimbriae, biofilm-associated pili, and cell wall-anchoring proteins can also increase biofilm formation. Type III fimbriae encoded as accessory proteins on conjugative plasmids have been demonstrated to mediate cell–cell and cell-surface adhesion<sup><a href="#CR439" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR439">439</a></sup>, as well as increased biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR440" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR440">440</a></sup> in <em>K. pneumoniae</em><sup><a href="#CR441" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR441">441</a></sup>. Further evidence of this was demonstrated in a uropathogenic strain of <em>E. coli</em> where Tn5 mutagenesis was used to identify biofilm-deficient mutants<sup><a href="#CR442" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR442">442</a></sup>. All Tn5 insertions were found to be within the type III fimbriae genes (<em>mrkABCDF</em>) encoded on the conjugative plasmid pMAS2027. This suggests that type III fimbriae were necessary for biofilm formation which was confirmed when this locus was cloned into biofilm-deficient mutants and was able to restore biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR442" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR442">442</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par89">When analyzed in a space environment it appears that pili and fimbriae contribute to increased biofilm formation in the short term but decreased biofilm formation in the long term. In a spaceflight study, researchers compared biofilm formation of <em>Acinetobacter schindleri</em> over a short duration (15 days) and long-duration (64 days) from the Shenzhou-10 spacecraft and Tiangong-2 space lab, respectively<sup><a href="#CR443" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR443">443</a></sup>. They observed reduced biofilm formation after 64 days and following transcriptional analysis proposed that this reduction was potentially due to downregulation of the <em>pil</em> and <em>algR</em> genes associated with conjugative pili and alginate biosynthesis, or upregulation of genes involved in metal iron binding (as available iron increases biofilm formation)<sup><a href="#CR443" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR443">443</a></sup>. In another study analyzing <em>Proteus mirabilis</em> biofilm formation following short-term or long-term SMG, researchers found that long-term exposure resulted in downregulation of genes associated with fimbriae, impeding adhesion, and ultimately decreasing biofilm formation<sup><a href="#CR444" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR444">444</a></sup>.</p>
<p id="Par90">The rate of HGT amongst bacterial communities in biofilms is increased as it contains a diversity of bacteria, the structured extracellular matrix (ECM) provides the ideal environment to stabilize mating pair formation and bacterial contact for genetic exchange, and eDNA accumulates within the ECM allowing for natural transformation<sup><a href="#CR435" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR435">435</a>,<a href="#CR445" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR445">445</a>–<a href="#CR447" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR447">447</a></sup>. Lécuyer et al. analyzed the conjugative transfer of an integrative and conjugative element (ICE) between <em>B. subtilis</em> strains on normal media compared to biofilm-inducing media. Results indicated that conjugative transfer was increased by 100-fold and 10,000-fold on biofilm-inducing media using minimal media and rich media, respectively<sup><a href="#CR446" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR446">446</a></sup>. Conjugation experiments performed between <em>S. aureus</em> strains using donors harboring a mobilizable plasmid and the multi-drug resistant conjugative plasmid, pGO1 yielded similar results<sup><a href="#CR448" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR448">448</a></sup>. Conjugation was performed with standard filter mating or a cellulose disk static biofilm model and conjugation frequency in the biofilm-promoting conditions was found to be ~16,000-fold higher than in standard conditions<sup><a href="#CR448" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR448">448</a></sup>. Overall, HGT promotes biofilm formation which, in turn, promotes HGT transfer, and both can contribute to increased bacterial pathogenicity (Fig. <a href="#Fig4" class="usa-link">4</a>). If HGT is increased under space conditions which can consequently lead to the production of more biofilms, the effect that biofilms have on spacecraft integrity and function could be even more severe during long-duration space travel if this feedback loop goes uninterrupted.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="Fig4"><h5 class="obj_head">Fig. 4. Interplay between bacterial HGT, biofilms, and virulence in space.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10284894_41526_2023_285_Fig4_HTML.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/ad26/10284894/5dfcaedc1d5c/41526_2023_285_Fig4_HTML.jpg" loading="lazy" id="d32e3890" height="779" width="748" alt="Fig. 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/Fig4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>In microgravity, bacterial HGT can increase cell–cell adhesion, and production of eDNA and extracellular polysaccharides contributing to increased biofilm formation. Reciprocally, biofilms promote HGT as they contain high microbial diversity and an ECM structure allowing for increased cell aggregation, stable mating pair formation and eDNA accumulation. Both HGT and biofilms increase bacterial virulence through cellular adhesion and dissemination of antimicrobial resistance, and increased biomass and resistance to disinfectants, respectively. Created with BioRender.com.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="Sec36"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Harnessing HGT as a tool for genetic engineering</h4>
<p id="Par91">Although HGT has the potential to negatively impact astronaut health and jeopardize spacecraft during long-duration space missions, conjugation machinery can also be harnessed as a tool for the development of novel mitigation strategies. Conjugation has been demonstrated within and between many species of Gram-negative and Gram-positive bacteria in a laboratory setting, and from bacteria to eukaryotic cells. Conjugative tool development for many of these species could have implications for their use in the space environment including those developed to target opportunistic pathogens (e.g., <em>Enterococcus</em>, <em>Listeria, Staphylococcus</em><sup><a href="#CR449" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR449">449</a></sup><em>, Streptomyces</em><sup><a href="#CR450" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR450">450</a></sup>, and <em>S. enterica</em><sup><a href="#CR451" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR451">451</a></sup>), or those that could be used to engineer useful strains such as probiotic candidates (e.g., <em>Bacillus</em><sup><a href="#CR449" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR449">449</a></sup>, <em>Bifidobacterium</em><sup><a href="#CR452" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR452">452</a></sup> and <em>Lactobacillus</em><sup><a href="#CR453" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR453">453</a></sup>) or extremophilic microbes (e.g., <em>D. radiodurans</em><sup><a href="#CR454" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR454">454</a></sup>, yeast<sup><a href="#CR455" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR455">455</a></sup> or algae<sup><a href="#CR267" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR267">267</a>,<a href="#CR268" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR268">268</a></sup>). When coupled with CRISPR/Cas technologies conjugation can be used for the modulation of microbiomes and as a method for targeted eradication of human pathogens on Earth. This has been demonstrated using a <em>cis</em>- and <em>trans</em>- conjugative plasmid encoding the I-TevI nuclease domain fused to <em>Streptococcus pyogenes</em> or <em>S. aureus</em> Cas9 (TevSpCas9 or TevSaCas9) targeting <em>S. enterica</em><sup><a href="#CR451" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR451">451</a></sup>. This study demonstrated <em>S. enterica</em> killing efficiencies ranging from 1 to 100% following conjugation using 65 single or multiplexed sgRNAs, allowing for the ability to modulate bacterial populations based on the chosen sgRNA. Another study using a similar strategy and a <em>trans</em>-conjugative system has demonstrated specific bacterial killing and targeting of a plasmid-born carbapenem resistance gene in <em>E. coli</em><sup><a href="#CR456" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR456">456</a></sup>. Other Cas nucleases can be used to target pathogens as well, including CRISPR/Cas13a-based antibacterial nucleocapsids, which have been shown to kill resistant <em>E. coli</em> and <em>S. aureus</em> species by targeting AMR genes<sup><a href="#CR457" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR457">457</a></sup>. CRISPR/Cas technologies can also be used to inhibit conjugation in human pathogens<sup><a href="#CR458" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR458">458</a>,<a href="#CR459" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR459">459</a></sup>, including species that have been isolated from the ISS such as <em>K. pneumonia</em><sup><a href="#CR460" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR460">460</a></sup>, <em>E. faecalis</em><sup><a href="#CR461" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR461">461</a></sup>, and <em>Staphylococcus</em><sup><a href="#CR462" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR462">462</a></sup>. The biotechnological applications of CRISPR/Cas systems in space are extensive, from using Cas proteins to understand the biological impact of microgravity to detecting the presence of pathogenic bacteria or viruses, as demonstrated on Earth using Cas12 or Cas13 for SARS-CoV-2 RNA detection<sup><a href="#CR463" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR463">463</a>–<a href="#CR465" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR465">465</a></sup>. The use of CRISPR/Cas systems for these in-space applications is feasible as genome editing using a CRISPR-based assay has been performed in <em>S. cerevisiae</em> onboard the ISS<sup><a href="#CR466" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR466">466</a></sup>. The mitigation strategies discussed here will require further testing and adaptation for space which could be carried out in synthetic human gut microbiomes<sup><a href="#CR467" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR467">467</a></sup> or in simulated spaceflight biofilms<sup><a href="#CR394" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR394">394</a></sup>. However, these results indicate that harnessing the power of conjugation and CRISPR/Cas technologies could provide a promising strategy for detection of pathogenic bacteria, mitigation of HGT and modulation of human and environmental microbiomes in spacecraft.</p></section></section><section id="Sec37"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Mitigation strategies</h3>
<p id="Par92">To minimize the health risks associated with extended spaceflight, it is critical that methods for preventing and treating spaceflight-induced illnesses be developed before astronauts embark upon long-duration space missions. Specifically, increased HGT, pathogenicity and biofilm formation by microorganisms in the space environment presents the need for highly effective antimicrobials and targeted solutions for use on spacecraft. Aside from traditional antibiotics<sup><a href="#CR468" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR468">468</a></sup>, the use of live engineered organisms, and the use of the conjugation and CRISPR-based strategies discussed above, other mitigation strategies have been investigated to inhibit HGT and the development of biofilms<sup><a href="#CR394" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR394">394</a></sup>. One well-studied method is antibacterial photodynamic inactivation employing the use of a photoactive compound which accumulates in target bacteria where it is illuminated to produce a cytotoxic reaction with oxygen resulting in cellular damage and bacterial death (reviewed in ref. <sup><a href="#CR469" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR469">469</a></sup>). Additional examples of mitigation strategies include the use of specialized material coatings or surfaces (e.g., copper-based antimicrobial surfaces<sup><a href="#CR470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR470">470</a>,<a href="#CR471" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR471">471</a></sup>, silver and ruthenium bio-deterrent surfaces<sup><a href="#CR472" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR472">472</a></sup>), engineered lantibiotics<sup><a href="#CR473" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR473">473</a></sup>, chemical water treatment, UV light, sound waves, and phenols<sup><a href="#CR474" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR474">474</a></sup>. Other promising antimicrobial materials include AGXX<sup><a href="#CR472" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR472">472</a></sup>, which was shown to prevent the growth of <em>Staphylococcus</em> and <em>Enterococcus</em> pathogens on the ISS, and sterilization using cold atmospheric plasma<sup><a href="#CR441" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR441">441</a></sup>. Some potential antibiofilm coating issues that need to be explored include the longevity and chemical stability of the coatings<sup><a href="#CR475" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR475">475</a></sup>. While several coatings give extremely promising results in the short term, their chemical and physical stability may diminish over time<sup><a href="#CR476" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR476">476</a></sup>. Regarding long-term space exploration, replenishing these antimicrobial coatings will prove challenging. The use of plant-based extracts is thus gaining in popularity for their antimicrobial and antibiofilm properties along with the added benefit of being able to harvest the necessary compounds during spaceflight, as needed<sup><a href="#CR390" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="CR390">390</a></sup>.</p></section></section><section id="Sec38"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Future outlook and summary</h2>
<p id="Par93">Within the next decade, great strides will be made in space exploration with the combined efforts of NASA and other space agencies to reach the Moon and Mars, as well as the expansion of commercialized spaceflight. The Artemis missions will work towards establishing a lunar base camp by 2025 with the purpose of utilizing the Moon as a precursor to Mars exploration. These missions will involve long-duration spaceflight and prolonged extraterrestrial occupation, requiring further exploration of health risks and resource sustainability than what was needed for the decades spent in LEO on the ISS. This review has discussed the role of microbes and their beneficial or detrimental impacts on long-duration space missions as demonstrated in Earth-based simulated space environments or experiments conducted in spaceflight. Microbes heavily contribute to the success of our ecosystem on Earth and can therefore be repurposed for the space environment to help generate a sustainable habitat. We have summarized the advantageous properties of microbes in plant cultivation, wastewater treatment, solid and liquid waste reuse, and atmospheric revitalization. This review has also considered the use of microbial fuel cells to generate energy from waste, plant decay, and solar light conversion. Although more research is needed for efficient energy conversion in the space environment, we present microbial fuel cells as an ideal and possible future alternative for energy generation. We also examined the advantages of bioengineered microbes for in-flight therapeutics, increased production of nutrients and oxygen, and the creation or recycling of infrastructure materials. Most of these microbial technologies have been successfully demonstrated on Earth, but as suggested, should be further tested, and applied in the space environment. Microbes are ideal candidates to aid in human survival during long-term space travel due to their many beneficial characteristics, ease of manipulation, and microscopic size, allowing them to be easily stowed without the concern of added weight. In terms of health and safety risks, the possibility of increased microbial virulence and pathogenicity in spaceflight was discussed, as well as the detrimental effects of increased HGT and biofilm formation on astronaut health and equipment integrity. Suggestions were also made regarding mitigation strategies for further investigation, including antimicrobial metallic surface materials and coatings that prevent increased HGT or biofilm formation. This review has presented the vast contributions that microbes can make towards future crewed missions, human habitability and sustainability and hopefully encourages more space-related microbial research interconnected with technological development, to continue to safely advance deep space exploration in the upcoming decade.</p></section><section id="ack1" class="ack"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Acknowledgements</h2>

All authors reviewed the final draft and approved it for submission.</p></section><section id="FPar1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Competing interests</h2>
